# QiServer

## Overview
- **Date**: 2026-04-08 19:45:27
- **Conversation ID**: `69d6f699-0734-832e-afba-81c7390bc948`
- **Source**: `Chat-Export-28513-2026/conversations-005.json`

## Chat History

### User
> SO YOU MEAN TO TELL ME I CAN RUN DOCKER AND OLLAMA ON MY EXTRA MACHINE USE TUNNELS WITH CLOUDFLARE AND THATS MY SERVER?

### Assistant
> Yes. Functionally, yes.
> 
> That extra machine **is your server** the moment it is running services for you or other devices over a network.
> 
> What you’re describing is basically this:
> 
> - **Extra machine** = the physical host
> - **Docker** = runs your apps cleanly in containers
> - **Ollama** = one of those apps, serving local AI models
> - **Cloudflare Tunnel** = gives outside access without opening router ports
> - **Your domain/subdomain** = clean front door to reach it
> 
> So in plain English:
> 
> **You run your stuff on the extra machine, and Cloudflare makes it reachable.**
> That is absolutely a legit self-hosted server setup.
> 
> What it is **not**:
> - not a magic enterprise-grade infra stack
> - not automatically high-availability
> - not immune to power outages, ISP issues, disk failure, or bad config
> - not something you should expose sloppily
> 
> The clean version is:
> 
> **Home/office box → Docker containers → reverse proxy/app layer → Cloudflare Tunnel → your domain**
> 
> For AI specifically:
> - Run **Ollama locally**
> - Keep it **behind auth**
> - Ideally do **not** expose raw Ollama directly to the public internet
> - Put a UI or API layer in front of it, like Open WebUI, LiteLLM, or your own app
> 
> The real caveats:
> - If the machine is weak, Ollama will be slow
> - If the machine restarts or sleeps, your “server” disappears
> - If storage isn’t planned, containers become chaos fast
> - If you rely on it for important operations, you need backups and basic monitoring
> 
> So the honest answer is:
> 
> **Yes, that counts as your server.**
> For a personal stack, internal tools, client portal experiments, private apps, and self-hosted AI, it’s a very solid move.
> 
> The sharper framing is:
> 
> **It’s not “pretend server.” It is a real server. It’s just a self-hosted single-node server.**
> 
> A practical starter stack for you would be:
> 
> - **Docker**
> - **Ollama**
> - **Open WebUI**
> - **Cloudflare Tunnel**
> - **Caddy or Traefik**
> - **Watchtower** for container updates
> - persistent volumes for every app
> - Tailscale too, if you want private admin access separate from public routes
> 
> Best mental model:
> 
> **Cloudflare Tunnel is the bridge. Docker is the organizer. Ollama is one workload. The machine is the server.**
> 
> Your next decision is not “is this a server?”
> It’s:
> 
> **Do you want this to be**
> 1. private AI box,
> 2. internal ops server,
> 3. client-facing app host,
> or
> 4. all three with proper separation?
> 
> If you want, I’ll map you a dead-simple architecture for this that fits your QiOS style instead of generic nerd soup.

### User
> i want it to server my own app and ai memory llm so that way i dont have to worry about overages and most of the harddrive can just be super smart ai modelws

### Assistant
> You can do it, but your current framing needs one correction: **big hard drives do not magically make AI good**. Storage helps hold models; actual performance comes from CPU, RAM, and especially GPU/VRAM.

### Assistant
> The right version is: **use the box as your private compute node, not as a junk drawer full of giant models**. For your goal, the win is one stable app stack plus one reliable local AI stack, then expose only the parts that should be reachable.

### Assistant
> Yes — **that is the right direction** for your use case, with one correction:
> 
> **Do not think “big hard drive = strong AI.”**  
> Big storage just means you can *store* a lot of models. It does **not** mean they will run well. Ollama can run locally, and model storage can consume tens to hundreds of GB, but speed and usefulness come from the machine’s compute: CPU, RAM, and especially GPU/VRAM. citeturn241678search4turn206514search9turn241678search1
> 
> The practical version of what you want is:
> 
> - **One machine = app host + AI host**
> - **Docker** runs your services cleanly
> - **Ollama** runs local models and exposes a local API on `localhost:11434` by default citeturn241678search4turn206514search5
> - **Your app** runs in its own container
> - **Cloudflare Tunnel** publishes selected local services without opening inbound ports on your router citeturn241678search0turn241678search3turn241678search18
> - **Cloudflare Access** sits in front of exposed apps as an identity-aware gatekeeper citeturn206514search16turn206514search8turn206514search2
> 
> So yes: you can use that extra machine to serve **your own app** and **your own local AI stack**, which means you are no longer paying a cloud provider per request/token for those local model calls. That does **not** remove cost entirely — you still pay in hardware, electricity, maintenance, and performance ceilings. That last part is inference from the fact that the models are running on your machine rather than a metered hosted API. citeturn241678search10turn241678search7
> 
> For the “AI memory” part, don’t confuse **chat history** with **retrieval memory**.  
> If you want real memory, the clean pattern is:
> 
> 1. store your notes/docs/chunks,
> 2. generate embeddings locally with Ollama,
> 3. save those vectors in a database,
> 4. retrieve relevant chunks into the prompt when needed.
> 
> Ollama supports embeddings directly, and its docs explicitly frame them for semantic search, retrieval, and RAG. citeturn206514search1turn206514search3
> 
> The stack I’d recommend for you is this:
> 
> **Core**
> - Docker
> - Your app container
> - Postgres
> - a vector-capable store for memory
> - Ollama
> 
> **AI layer**
> - one fast daily-driver model
> - one stronger reasoning/coding model
> - one embedding model
> 
> **Access layer**
> - Cloudflare Tunnel
> - Cloudflare Access
> - optional custom subdomains like `app.yourdomain.com` and `ai.yourdomain.com`
> 
> **UI**
> - Open WebUI if you want a ready-made chat/admin interface; it supports Ollama, can run offline, and its Docker path is the recommended quick start. citeturn241678search5turn241678search8turn241678search2
> 
> The important design rule:
> 
> **Do not expose raw Ollama directly if you can avoid it.**  
> By default, local access to Ollama’s API does not require authentication. That is fine on localhost; it is a bad habit for public exposure. Put Cloudflare Access or your own authenticated API in front of anything reachable from the internet. citeturn206514search11turn206514search16
> 
> Also, don’t waste your drive on a graveyard of giant models you never use. That’s hoarder architecture. Better is:
> 
> - 1 **fast small model** for routing, summarizing, simple ops
> - 1 **stronger model** for heavier reasoning/coding
> - 1 **embedding model** for memory/RAG
> 
> That setup is usually more useful than stuffing a disk with fifteen massive models and pretending you built intelligence.
> 
> Here’s the blunt truth about feasibility:
> 
> - If that extra machine has **no decent GPU**, it can still work, but large local models will be slow.
> - If it has **good RAM and a real GPU**, this becomes much more viable.
> - If you want it to serve both your app and AI reliably, keep them in separate containers and budget resources so the LLM doesn’t choke your app.
> 
> Best architecture for your goal:
> 
> **public app**
> - browser → Cloudflare → Tunnel → app container
> 
> **private/controlled AI**
> - browser/app → Cloudflare Access → AI UI or your own API → Ollama → vector DB/postgres
> 
> That gives you:
> - one machine
> - one domain front door
> - your own app hosting
> - your own local LLM
> - your own retrieval memory
> - far less dependence on usage-based cloud AI billing
> 
> The only part you should not do is this:  
> **“Most of the hard drive can just be super smart AI models.”**  
> No. That’s not strategy. That’s storage cosplay.
> 
> What you actually want is:
> - a **small, curated model set**
> - a **good memory pipeline**
> - **tight access control**
> - **persistent volumes/backups**
> - **resource separation**
> 
> If you want, I’ll lay out the exact Docker stack and folder structure for this in a QiOS-style way: containers, subdomains, volumes, and which services should be public vs private.

### User
> ok so what is the setup we need to do should i wipe the computer start fresh, create another user to run this on and just leave the others dormit just incase.

### Assistant
> I’m checking the official docs so I don’t hand you stale setup advice. Then I’ll give you the cleanest path: keep, wipe, or split-user.

### Assistant
> I’ve got the official pieces. The setup is straightforward; the real choice is whether this box is a test toy or actual infrastructure. For actual infrastructure, the answer is cleaner than you think.

### Assistant
> Yes. For a machine you want to trust as **real infrastructure**, my recommendation is:
> 
> **Back it up, wipe it, install fresh, and dedicate it to server duty.**  
> Docker Engine is well-supported on Linux, Docker documents running it as a non-root user and enabling it on boot, Ollama’s Linux docs explicitly include adding it as a startup service, and Cloudflare Tunnel is designed to run a local `cloudflared` daemon that makes outbound-only connections from your origin machine. citeturn473709search0turn473709search12turn876589search1turn473709search5turn473709search8
> 
> What I **would not** do is use “leave the old users dormant” as your safety plan. That is messy rollback theater. Better rollback is: **full backup or disk image first**, then clean install. Dormant old profiles just leave behind junk, permissions weirdness, random startup crap, stale drivers, and mystery disk usage.
> 
> My blunt answer:
> 
> **If this box is becoming your app + AI server, wipe it.**  
> **If this box is only for testing and you’re not committed yet, don’t wipe — create a dedicated user and prove the stack first.**
> 
> Best production path:
> 
> 1. **Inventory and back up the machine first.**  
>    Confirm CPU, RAM, GPU, SSD size, and whether you care about anything still on it. Do not nuke first and think later.
> 
> 2. **Fresh install Linux.**  
>    I’d use **Ubuntu Server LTS** or **Debian** for this job. Docker’s Linux install path is the clean server route; Docker Desktop is a different product and mostly for desktop workflows. citeturn473709search0turn473709search3turn473709search6
> 
> 3. **Create one real admin user.**  
>    Something like `cody-admin`.  
>    Not five users. Not old profiles hanging around. One admin account, SSH access, clean host.
> 
> 4. **Install Docker Engine and Compose.**  
>    Then do Docker’s post-install steps so you can run Docker without living as root all day and have services start properly on boot. citeturn473709search12turn473709search15
> 
> 5. **Run Ollama on the host first.**  
>    Not because it can’t be containerized, but because for v1 it is simpler to troubleshoot GPU/driver issues and Ollama’s Linux path already supports running as a startup service. Also, Ollama’s local API is available once it’s running. citeturn876589search1turn876589search2
> 
> 6. **Run your app stack in Docker.**  
>    App container, database container, reverse proxy if needed, and persistent volumes. Keep app data and model data on separate storage paths so you don’t create a future garbage fire.
> 
> 7. **Only after local works, add Cloudflare Tunnel.**  
>    Cloudflare Tunnel is for publishing services without exposing a public IP or opening inbound ports; `cloudflared` runs on the server and connects outbound to Cloudflare. Do not start with internet exposure before the stack works locally. citeturn473709search5turn473709search8turn473709search11
> 
> 8. **Put auth in front of anything sensitive.**  
>    Especially AI endpoints. Ollama’s docs state that no authentication is required for local access to `http://localhost:11434`, which is fine for localhost and stupid for raw public exposure. Put Cloudflare Access or your own auth layer in front of anything exposed. citeturn876589search0turn473709search2
> 
> Here’s the version where you **do not** wipe:
> 
> - Keep the existing OS
> - Create a dedicated local user just for server duties
> - Install Docker/Ollama under that user
> - Keep everything local-only at first
> - Treat it as a pilot, not production
> 
> That path is most justified on **Windows**, because Ollama’s Windows install goes into the user’s home directory by default, so a dedicated account keeps that install more contained. citeturn876589search15
> 
> So the decision rule is simple:
> 
> **Wipe it** if:
> - this will be a dedicated box
> - you want reliability
> - the machine already has random life junk on it
> - you don’t want to debug old-user ghosts later
> 
> **Do not wipe yet** if:
> - you are still hardware-testing
> - you are unsure the machine is strong enough
> - you want a 1–2 day proof-of-concept before committing
> 
> My recommendation for you, based on what you said you want:
> 
> **Phase 1:** backup + hardware inventory  
> **Phase 2:** fresh Linux install  
> **Phase 3:** Docker + Ollama local-only  
> **Phase 4:** app running locally  
> **Phase 5:** Cloudflare Tunnel + Access  
> **Phase 6:** memory/RAG layer and curated model storage
> 
> And no, I would not keep multiple old human users “just in case.”  
> That is desktop thinking, not server thinking.
> 
> Send me the machine specs — **CPU, RAM, GPU, SSD/HDD sizes, and current OS** — and I’ll tell you whether this should be a clean Linux server box or a temporary Windows pilot.

### Assistant
> Perfect. Then **wipe it**.
> 
> That removes the last excuse to do this halfway.
> 
> **Do not keep dormant old users. Do not keep the old install. Do not “maybe later” the cleanup.**  
> Make it a dedicated server box from day one.
> 
> Here’s the setup I’d do.
> 
> ## What to install
> 
> Use **Ubuntu Server 24.04 LTS** unless you have a strong reason not to. Docker officially supports Ubuntu 24.04 LTS, and Ubuntu Server keeps the machine lean instead of wasting resources on desktop fluff. citeturn446210search0turn446210search4
> 
> ## How to structure the machine
> 
> Create:
> 
> - **one admin user** for you
> - a clear hostname, like `qinode01` or `qiserver01`
> - SSH enabled during install
> 
> Then after install:
> 
> - install **Docker Engine**
> - do Docker’s post-install steps so you can run Docker without living as root and have Docker start on boot citeturn446210search12turn446210search4
> - install **Ollama**
> - set Ollama to run as a startup service, which Ollama explicitly recommends on Linux citeturn446210search1
> 
> ## The order matters
> 
> Do it in this order:
> 
> 1. **Wipe and fresh install Ubuntu Server**
> 2. **Update the system fully**
> 3. **Install SSH**
> 4. **Install Docker Engine**
> 5. **Configure Docker post-install**
> 6. **Install Ollama**
> 7. **Test Ollama locally**
> 8. **Deploy your app stack locally with Docker Compose**
> 9. **Only after local works, install Cloudflare Tunnel**
> 10. **Put Cloudflare Access/auth in front of exposed services**
> 
> That last part matters because Ollama’s API is available locally once running, and by default it binds to `127.0.0.1:11434`. That is good. Keep it that way until you deliberately front it with your own app or an access layer. citeturn446210search21turn446210search5
> 
> ## What not to do
> 
> Do **not**:
> 
> - expose raw Ollama directly to the internet
> - install random desktop junk
> - create multiple “just in case” users
> - use this same box for messy personal browsing
> - fill the drive with every model you can find
> 
> That is how people turn a server into a haunted attic.
> 
> ## The clean deployment model
> 
> Use the machine like this:
> 
> - **Host OS**: Ubuntu Server
> - **Docker**: your app, database, helper services
> - **Ollama**: local model runtime
> - **Cloudflare Tunnel**: publishes selected services without opening inbound ports on your router citeturn446210search22turn446210search10
> - **Cloudflare Access**: auth wall in front of anything private
> 
> ## If the machine has a GPU
> 
> Then after Ubuntu install, check the GPU before going further.
> 
> If it’s **NVIDIA**:
> - install the Linux NVIDIA driver
> - then install **NVIDIA Container Toolkit** if you want GPU-accelerated Docker containers; NVIDIA’s docs list the driver as a prerequisite and the toolkit as the way to run GPU-accelerated containers. citeturn446210search15turn446210search23
> 
> If it’s **AMD**:
> - Ollama’s hardware support docs point to ROCm on Linux for AMD GPU support. citeturn446210search17turn446210search1
> 
> If it has **no useful GPU**, the setup still works, but you need to be realistic: your app will be fine, your LLM layer will just be slower.
> 
> ## Directory layout I’d use
> 
> On the server, make it boring and obvious:
> 
> - `/srv/docker/` → all Docker app stacks
> - `/srv/docker/app/`
> - `/srv/docker/postgres/`
> - `/srv/docker/openwebui/` if you use it
> - `/srv/ollama/` → Ollama data/model storage
> - `/srv/backups/` → backups and exports
> 
> That keeps app data, AI data, and backups from getting mixed into nonsense.
> 
> ## My actual recommendation
> 
> Your move is:
> 
> **Yes, wipe it tonight.**  
> Then install **Ubuntu Server 24.04 LTS**, one admin user, SSH, Docker, Ollama, local app stack, then Cloudflare Tunnel last. Docker documents the Ubuntu install path and Linux post-install; Ollama documents Linux install and startup service; Cloudflare documents `cloudflared` as the daemon that connects your origin server to Cloudflare and supports running it as a Linux service. citeturn446210search0turn446210search12turn446210search1turn446210search2turn446210search6
> 
> The only thing I still need from you to narrow the AI side correctly is:
> 
> **CPU, RAM, GPU, and drive sizes.**
> 
> Because that determines whether this is:
> - app host + small local models,
> - app host + decent AI box,
> - or app host + serious model node.

### User
> wait i am remoting into this pc, and i will lose connection if i wipe it, look the users profiles on here are not taking up any space really. do we really need to wipe it.

### Assistant
> No. **Do not wipe a machine you only have through remote access unless you already have hands-on recovery lined up.** That would be a dumb self-own for almost no gain here.

### Assistant
> No. **You do not need to wipe it right now.**
> 
> Given the new constraint, wiping is the wrong move.
> 
> You’re remote. You’ll lose access. The old profiles aren’t consuming meaningful space. There’s nothing materially wrong you’re solving by nuking it today. That makes a wipe **high risk, low reward**.
> 
> What to do instead:
> 
> 1. **Keep the current OS**
> 2. **Use this as a staged pilot server**
> 3. **Create one dedicated folder structure for server stuff**
> 4. **Install and test everything locally on this machine**
> 5. **Only plan a wipe later if the machine proves worth formalizing**
> 
> That is the grown-up move. Not “clean install because it feels pure.”
> 
> ## My recommendation now
> 
> ### Option A — fastest and most practical
> Use the current account for setup, but keep your server stuff isolated:
> 
> - `C:\QiServer\docker\`
> - `C:\QiServer\app\`
> - `C:\QiServer\ollama\`
> - `C:\QiServer\data\`
> - `C:\QiServer\backups\`
> 
> This is the simplest path if the box is basically empty.
> 
> ### Option B — cleaner separation
> Create **one new local admin user** just for server management and remote into that account going forward.
> 
> Do this only if you want separation.  
> Do **not** create multiple users “just in case.” One is enough.
> 
> If the current machine is clean and stable, Option A is fine for now.
> 
> ## What matters more than wiping
> 
> Right now, the real risks are not “old user profiles.”
> 
> The real risks are:
> 
> - machine goes to sleep
> - automatic restart kills services
> - storage gets messy
> - Docker/Ollama/app data all end up mixed together
> - you expose something before auth is in place
> 
> That’s what you should control first.
> 
> ## Your immediate setup path
> 
> Do this first:
> 
> ### 1. Lock down power behavior
> Make sure the PC:
> 
> - never sleeps
> - never hibernates
> - stays on when idle
> - doesn’t aggressively auto-reboot without you knowing
> 
> Because a “server” that naps is not a server. It’s a flaky roommate.
> 
> ### 2. Give it a real identity
> Set:
> 
> - a clear computer name like `QISERVER01`
> - a static local IP if possible
> - a note somewhere with its login info and purpose
> 
> ### 3. Create clean storage boundaries
> Even on Windows, keep it organized:
> 
> - `C:\QiServer\docker`
> - `C:\QiServer\volumes`
> - `C:\QiServer\ollama`
> - `C:\QiServer\logs`
> - `C:\QiServer\backups`
> 
> ### 4. Test the stack before making it “official”
> Install your stack and prove:
> 
> - Docker works
> - Ollama runs
> - your app runs
> - remote access is stable
> - the machine doesn’t choke under load
> 
> ### 5. Reassess later
> After it’s working, then decide:
> 
> - keep current OS and formalize it
> - or schedule a wipe when you have physical access
> 
> That is the right sequence.
> 
> ## So do we really need to wipe it?
> 
> **No. Not now.**
> Not with remote-only access.
> Not for tiny profile savings.
> Not unless the current install is unstable, bloated, compromised, or chaotic.
> 
> A wipe is a **phase-2 cleanup move**, not a prerequisite.
> 
> ## What I’d do in your position
> 
> I would:
> 
> - leave the machine as-is
> - make one clean server root folder
> - install the stack
> - prove the box can handle the workload
> - only wipe later if I decide this is becoming permanent infrastructure
> 
> That gets you moving **today** without cutting off your own access.
> 
> The next thing to decide is this:
> 
> **Are you keeping Windows for the pilot, or do you already know this box is eventually becoming Linux-only?**
> 
> Because that changes the exact install path.

### Assistant
> I’m checking current self-hosted options so I don’t hand you a dead project or a bloated one. Goal is simple: one queue, visible status, controlled intake, low admin drag.

### Assistant
> I found the fork in the road. You are asking for two jobs at once: **visibility** and **intake**. Most tools do one well and the other awkwardly.

### User
> i have notion, i could make them use that.

### Assistant
> Yes — and for **your actual need**, Notion is probably the fastest answer. I’m verifying which pieces are officially built in now so I can tell you whether this is enough by itself or needs one add-on.

### Assistant
> Yes. **Use Notion for this first.**
> 
> For what you described — “a queue people can see, a simple intake, and a way to stop random drops on me” — Notion is good enough **right now**. It already gives you databases, multiple views, task/status structure, automations, public pages/sites, and even public forms inside the Notion publishing system. It also now has task databases with status/assignee/due date structure and optional ID properties, which is exactly the skeleton you need for a lightweight ticket queue. citeturn833727search0turn833727search1turn833727search2turn744177search4turn744177search11
> 
> The main reason I’d say yes is this: **your problem is governance, not tooling**. You do not need a fancy helpdesk yet. You need one visible pipeline and one intake lane. Notion can do that without you spinning up another system to babysit. It supports multiple database layouts like table, board, list, and calendar, and you can publish pages publicly or share them with collaborators depending on how open you want the queue to be. citeturn744177search9turn111065search12turn744177search11turn111065search4
> 
> What I would build is very simple:
> 
> **One database called `Queue`** with these fields:
> - Title
> - Status
> - Priority
> - Requested by
> - Type
> - Due date
> - Next action
> - Waiting on
> - Notes
> - ID
> 
> Use Notion’s task database format so you get proper status/assignee/due-date behavior, and add an ID property so each item has a clean reference number. Notion explicitly supports turning a database into a task database and adding unique IDs with optional prefixes. citeturn744177search4turn744177search7
> 
> Then make **three views only**:
> - `Submit / Request`
> - `Now`
> - `Queue / Backlog`
> 
> Maybe a fourth: `Waiting on Others`.
> 
> Do **not** build twelve views because that’s how you recreate your own chaos in prettier colors. Notion supports multiple views on the same database, which is useful here, but you still need restraint. citeturn833727search0turn111065search14turn111065search12
> 
> For access control, this is the smart move:
> - let others **submit**
> - let them **view status**
> - do **not** let everyone redesign the system
> 
> Notion has a “Can edit content” permission level specifically to let people edit entries without letting them wreck the database structure, views, filters, and property setup. That is exactly the permission model you want if other people are involved. citeturn744177search0
> 
> Your intake options are:
> 
> **Best simple option:** a Notion form feeding the Queue database.  
> Official help pages now explicitly reference public forms among published Notion pages, and Notion’s database docs also reference forms as a database capability. citeturn744177search11turn744177search3
> 
> **Fallback if forms are annoying in your plan/setup:** a shared page with a linked filtered database view plus a “New request” template button.  
> That is less elegant, but still workable because Notion databases are pages with views and templates. citeturn833727search0turn744177search14
> 
> The rule you need to enforce is:
> 
> **If it is not in the queue, it is not real.**
> 
> That’s the operating change. Notion is just the container.
> 
> My blunt recommendation:
> - **Yes, use Notion.**
> - Build one queue database.
> - Publish one intake page or form.
> - Give people visibility into status.
> - Stop accepting side-door task drops by DM/text unless you add them to the queue yourself.
> 
> Do **not** go shopping for a self-hosted ticket system yet unless you specifically need email-to-ticket, SLAs, customer portals, or more rigid support workflows. For your current problem, that would be premature nerd procrastination.
> 
> Here’s the cleanest setup:
> 
> **Top page:** `Work Queue`  
> Inside it:
> - a short “How to submit work” section
> - the intake form or new-request link
> - `Now` view
> - `Backlog` view
> - `Waiting` view
> - a small rules box:
>   - urgent means same-day operational blocker
>   - everything else goes in queue
>   - no random DMs for non-urgent work
> 
> If you want, I’ll map the exact Notion database schema and the rules text so you can build it in like 15 minutes.

### Assistant
> Yes. Here’s the fast, sane version.
> 
> **Do not use a Cloudflare quick tunnel for the AI UI.** Cloudflare says quick tunnels are for testing only, cap at 200 concurrent requests, and do not support SSE. Since AI chat UIs commonly stream responses, use a **real remotely managed tunnel** instead. Also, anyone holding the tunnel token can run that tunnel, so treat the token like a password. citeturn152638view3turn342627search2
> 
> The clean v1 stack on the other machine is:
> 
> - **cloudflared as a host service** on that machine
> - **Docker** for app services
> - **Ollama** for local models and embeddings
> - **Open WebUI** for human chat/admin access
> - **Neo4j** for graph + vector index
> - **one ingest worker later**, not five fake microservices
> 
> Open WebUI officially supports Ollama and OpenAI-compatible APIs, and Ollama’s API exposes `/api/embed` for embeddings. Neo4j supports vector indexes with `CREATE VECTOR INDEX`, and its official docs cover Docker and Docker Compose deployment. Cloudflare Access can sit in front as the identity-aware gate. citeturn152638view5turn458197search7turn844154search1turn844154search2turn646679view0turn523988search2turn152638view4
> 
> ### What I’d do tonight
> 
> 1. Put this on the **other machine**, not your main one.
> 2. Tunnel **only** the UI or your app.
> 3. Keep **Ollama and Neo4j private**.
> 4. Run ingestion/embedding there so your main machine stops eating that load.
> 
> ### Folder layout
> 
> Use something like:
> 
> ```text
> C:\QiServer\
>   compose\
>     docker-compose.yml
>     .env
>   volumes\
>     ollama\
>     open-webui\
>     neo4j-data\
>     neo4j-logs\
>     neo4j-plugins\
>   data\
>     inbox\
>     processed\
> ```
> 
> ### `docker-compose.yml`
> 
> This is the starter stack I’d use:
> 
> ```yaml
> services:
>   ollama:
>     image: ollama/ollama:latest
>     container_name: ollama
>     restart: unless-stopped
>     ports:
>       - "127.0.0.1:11434:11434"
>     volumes:
>       - ../volumes/ollama:/root/.ollama
> 
>   open-webui:
>     image: ghcr.io/open-webui/open-webui:main
>     container_name: open-webui
>     restart: unless-stopped
>     depends_on:
>       - ollama
>     environment:
>       OLLAMA_BASE_URL: http://ollama:11434
>       WEBUI_SECRET_KEY: ${WEBUI_SECRET_KEY}
>     ports:
>       - "127.0.0.1:3000:8080"
>     volumes:
>       - ../volumes/open-webui:/app/backend/data
> 
>   neo4j:
>     image: neo4j:latest
>     container_name: neo4j
>     restart: unless-stopped
>     environment:
>       NEO4J_AUTH: neo4j/${NEO4J_PASSWORD}
>     ports:
>       - "127.0.0.1:7474:7474"
>       - "127.0.0.1:7687:7687"
>     volumes:
>       - ../volumes/neo4j-data:/data
>       - ../volumes/neo4j-logs:/logs
>       - ../volumes/neo4j-plugins:/plugins
> 
> networks:
>   default:
>     name: ai-node
> ```
> 
> That gives you:
> 
> - Open WebUI at `http://localhost:3000`
> - Ollama API at `http://localhost:11434`
> - Neo4j browser at `http://localhost:7474`
> 
> I bound them to `127.0.0.1` on purpose so they stay local to that machine. Then **cloudflared** can publish only what you choose.
> 
> ### `.env`
> 
> ```env
> WEBUI_SECRET_KEY=replace_this_with_a_real_secret
> NEO4J_PASSWORD=replace_this_with_a_real_password
> ```
> 
> ### Bring it up
> 
> ```bash
> docker compose up -d
> ```
> 
> ### Pull one chat model and one embedding model
> 
> Ollama’s docs recommend embedding models like `embeddinggemma`, `qwen3-embedding`, and `all-minilm`. Use one embedding model and one normal chat model. citeturn152638view6turn844154search1
> 
> ```bash
> docker exec -it ollama ollama pull qwen3-embedding
> docker exec -it ollama ollama pull llama3.2
> ```
> 
> ### Cloudflare tunnel setup
> 
> Use a **real tunnel**, not quick tunnel.
> 
> In Cloudflare:
> 
> 1. Create a tunnel in **Networking → Tunnels**
> 2. Add a published app route like:
>    - `ai.yourdomain.com` → `http://localhost:3000`
> 3. Install `cloudflared` on that machine and run it as a service with the tunnel token
> 
> Cloudflare’s docs show the service install commands directly:
> - **Windows:** `cloudflared.exe service install <TUNNEL_TOKEN>`
> - **Linux:** `sudo cloudflared service install <TUNNEL_TOKEN>` citeturn152638view3
> 
> Then add a **Cloudflare Access self-hosted app** for `ai.yourdomain.com` so only approved people can get in. Cloudflare Access is explicitly an identity-aware proxy for self-hosted web apps. citeturn152638view4turn403756search0
> 
> ### Neo4j vector index
> 
> Neo4j supports vector indexes directly. Create chunk nodes with an `embedding` property, then build an index like this: citeturn646679view0
> 
> ```cypher
> CREATE VECTOR INDEX chunk_embedding IF NOT EXISTS
> FOR (c:Chunk)
> ON c.embedding
> OPTIONS { indexConfig: {
>   `vector.dimensions`: 768,
>   `vector.similarity_function`: 'cosine'
> }};
> ```
> 
> Use the correct dimension for the embedding model you actually picked. Neo4j recommends setting dimensions explicitly, and new vector indexes build in the background. citeturn646679view0
> 
> ### Worker strategy
> 
> Do **not** build a full queue stack tonight.
> 
> Start with **one worker process** on that other machine:
> 
> - watches `data/inbox`
> - extracts text
> - chunks text
> - calls `http://ollama:11434/api/embed`
> - writes:
>   - `(:Document)`
>   - `(:Chunk {text, embedding, source, hash})`
>   - relationships like `(:Document)-[:HAS_CHUNK]->(:Chunk)`
> 
> Ollama’s embedding API is `POST /api/embed`, and its local API is served by default at `http://localhost:11434/api`. citeturn844154search1turn844154search2
> 
> So your main machine becomes:
> - submitter
> - UI
> - maybe app client
> 
> And the other machine becomes:
> - model runtime
> - vectorization box
> - graph updater
> - ingestion worker node
> 
> ### GPU note
> 
> The compose above is the **safe baseline**. If that other machine has a GPU, Ollama’s official Docker docs support:
> - **NVIDIA** with `--gpus=all` after installing NVIDIA Container Toolkit
> - **AMD** with the `ollama/ollama:rocm` image and device mappings for `/dev/kfd` and `/dev/dri` citeturn241574view0turn241574view1turn241574view2
> 
> So don’t overcomplicate the first pass. Get the CPU-safe stack up, prove the tunnel, then GPU-optimize.
> 
> ### Best v1 boundary
> 
> Expose:
> - `ai.yourdomain.com` → Open WebUI or later your own app
> 
> Do **not** expose:
> - Neo4j
> - Ollama raw API
> - worker internals
> 
> That keeps the public surface small while the heavy lifting happens on the other machine.
> 
> Send me the other machine’s **OS + GPU**, and I’ll give you the exact next piece: either the GPU-ready Ollama service stanza or the first `worker.py` + worker Dockerfile for ingestion into Neo4j.

### Assistant
> Yes. This is the right move.
> 
> Your Ubuntu box should become the **private AI/ingest node**. Per your blueprint, the **local runtime** is where file watching, ingest, OCR, extraction, chunking, embeddings, and a local API live; the **cloud runtime** remains canonical metadata in Supabase with pgvector, while Neo4j stays derived and non-canonical. The ingest flow should assign canonical identity before extraction, then embed locally, then push vectors downstream. fileciteturn2file1
> 
> Here’s the order I’d use tonight:
> 
> ## 1) Put the server on Tailscale first
> 
> For Ubuntu/Linux, Tailscale’s install script is the supported quick path. After install, `tailscale up` gives you the auth URL, `tailscale ip` shows the tailnet IPs, and `tailscale status` verifies connectivity. You can also enable Tailscale SSH. For long-lived servers, Tailscale recommends using an auth key and a tag for provisioning. citeturn775328view0turn990623view1turn990623view2
> 
> Run this now:
> 
> ```bash
> curl -fsSL https://tailscale.com/install.sh | sh
> sudo tailscale up --ssh
> tailscale ip
> tailscale status
> ```
> 
> For the cleaner long-term server setup, do this from your Tailscale admin side later:
> - create a tag like `tag:server`
> - generate an auth key
> - re-run with hostname + tag
> 
> Tailscale’s documented server pattern is:
> 
> ```bash
> sudo tailscale up --auth-key=$TS_AUTHKEY --hostname=qinode01 --advertise-tags=tag:server --ssh
> ```
> 
> If the auth key already includes the tag, you can skip `--advertise-tags`. citeturn990623view2
> 
> Optional but smart: disable key expiry for this server once it’s trusted, because Tailscale explicitly calls out servers as a common case for that. citeturn990623view1
> 
> ## 2) Install Docker Engine, not Docker Desktop
> 
> Docker’s official Ubuntu path is the apt repo install. Docker also warns that published container ports can bypass `ufw`/`firewalld` rules, so don’t get cute and start exposing random ports to the LAN or WAN. citeturn777001view1turn777001view3
> 
> Run this:
> 
> ```bash
> sudo apt remove $(dpkg --get-selections docker.io docker-compose docker-compose-v2 docker-doc podman-docker containerd runc | cut -f1) 2>/dev/null || true
> 
> sudo apt update
> sudo apt install -y ca-certificates curl
> sudo install -m 0755 -d /etc/apt/keyrings
> sudo curl -fsSL https://download.docker.com/linux/ubuntu/gpg -o /etc/apt/keyrings/docker.asc
> sudo chmod a+r /etc/apt/keyrings/docker.asc
> 
> sudo tee /etc/apt/sources.list.d/docker.sources <<EOF
> Types: deb
> URIs: https://download.docker.com/linux/ubuntu
> Suites: $(. /etc/os-release && echo "${UBUNTU_CODENAME:-$VERSION_CODENAME}")
> Components: stable
> Architectures: $(dpkg --print-architecture)
> Signed-By: /etc/apt/keyrings/docker.asc
> EOF
> 
> sudo apt update
> sudo apt install -y docker-ce docker-ce-cli containerd.io docker-buildx-plugin docker-compose-plugin
> sudo systemctl status docker
> sudo docker run hello-world
> ```
> 
> Then make Docker usable without `sudo`:
> 
> ```bash
> sudo groupadd docker 2>/dev/null || true
> sudo usermod -aG docker $USER
> newgrp docker
> docker run hello-world
> ```
> 
> Docker documents that the `docker` group is effectively root-level access. That’s normal, just don’t hand it out casually. citeturn777001view0turn777001view2turn775328view4
> 
> ## 3) Create the Linux version of your QiData runtime
> 
> Your blueprint’s working directory includes `inbox`, `processing`, `reviewed`, `failed`, `manifests`, `extracted_text`, `embeddings_cache`, `logs`, and `model_cache`. On Linux, I’d map that to `/srv/qidata` and keep the QiOS runtime code under `/srv/qios`. fileciteturn2file1
> 
> Run:
> 
> ```bash
> sudo mkdir -p /srv/qidata/{inbox,processing,reviewed,failed,manifests,extracted_text,embeddings_cache,logs,model_cache,exports}
> sudo mkdir -p /srv/qios/{compose,python_local/{api,file_pipeline,services,jobs,config},workers}
> sudo chown -R $USER:$USER /srv/qidata /srv/qios
> ```
> 
> That gives you one clean place for:
> - hot inbox files
> - extracted text
> - embedding cache
> - logs
> - local pipeline code
> 
> ## 4) Install Ollama on the host
> 
> Ollama’s Linux install is straightforward, and on Linux it runs as a startup service. Its API is served by default at `http://localhost:11434/api`, and the embeddings endpoint is `POST /api/embed`. citeturn775328view5turn775328view6turn839110search7turn839110search1
> 
> Run:
> 
> ```bash
> curl -fsSL https://ollama.com/install.sh | sh
> ollama -v
> journalctl -e -u ollama
> ```
> 
> Pull one chat model and one embedding model:
> 
> ```bash
> ollama pull llama3.2
> ollama pull embeddinggemma
> ```
> 
> Quick API checks:
> 
> ```bash
> curl http://localhost:11434/api/generate -d '{
>   "model": "llama3.2",
>   "prompt": "Say hello from the Ubuntu AI node."
> }'
> 
> curl http://localhost:11434/api/embed -d '{
>   "model": "embeddinggemma",
>   "input": "This is a test embedding."
> }'
> ```
> 
> If you want Docker containers on the same machine to reach Ollama cleanly, Ollama’s docs say to set `OLLAMA_HOST=0.0.0.0:11434` via systemd on Linux. citeturn691366search1
> 
> Do that with:
> 
> ```bash
> sudo systemctl edit ollama.service
> ```
> 
> Add:
> 
> ```ini
> [Service]
> Environment="OLLAMA_HOST=0.0.0.0:11434"
> Environment="OLLAMA_CONTEXT_LENGTH=8192"
> ```
> 
> Then reload:
> 
> ```bash
> sudo systemctl daemon-reload
> sudo systemctl restart ollama
> ```
> 
> ## 5) Run the UI and graph services in Docker
> 
> Open WebUI’s Docker path is the officially recommended one for most users, and its quick-start docs include a Compose example. It also requires WebSocket support, which matters for chat streaming. Neo4j officially supports Docker Compose deployment and vector indexes. citeturn859157view0turn236133view2turn775328view8turn839110search0turn839110search3
> 
> Create `/srv/qios/compose/.env`:
> 
> ```env
> WEBUI_SECRET_KEY=replace_me_with_a_real_secret
> NEO4J_PASSWORD=replace_me_with_a_real_password
> ```
> 
> Create `/srv/qios/compose/docker-compose.yml`:
> 
> ```yaml
> services:
>   openwebui:
>     image: ghcr.io/open-webui/open-webui:main
>     container_name: open-webui
>     restart: unless-stopped
>     ports:
>       - "127.0.0.1:3000:8080"
>     environment:
>       WEBUI_SECRET_KEY: ${WEBUI_SECRET_KEY}
>       OLLAMA_BASE_URL: http://host.docker.internal:11434
>     extra_hosts:
>       - "host.docker.internal:host-gateway"
>     volumes:
>       - /srv/qios/open-webui:/app/backend/data
> 
>   neo4j:
>     image: neo4j:latest
>     container_name: neo4j
>     restart: unless-stopped
>     ports:
>       - "127.0.0.1:7474:7474"
>       - "127.0.0.1:7687:7687"
>     environment:
>       NEO4J_AUTH: neo4j/${NEO4J_PASSWORD}
>     volumes:
>       - /srv/qios/neo4j/data:/data
>       - /srv/qios/neo4j/logs:/logs
>       - /srv/qios/neo4j/plugins:/plugins
> ```
> 
> Why the `extra_hosts` line: Ollama’s own integration docs note that on Linux, `host.docker.internal` is not automatically available in containers, and you need to add `host-gateway` explicitly. citeturn691366search8
> 
> Bring it up:
> 
> ```bash
> cd /srv/qios/compose
> docker compose up -d
> docker ps
> ```
> 
> ## 6) Keep the UI private with Tailscale Serve
> 
> This is the clean move. Tailscale Serve can proxy a local service like `http://127.0.0.1:3000` to your tailnet, and Tailscale explicitly says it’s best practice for the backend service to listen on localhost only. `--bg` makes it persist across reboot/restart. citeturn993155view0turn993155view1
> 
> Run:
> 
> ```bash
> tailscale serve --bg 3000
> tailscale serve status
> ```
> 
> That gives you a private tailnet URL for Open WebUI without making it public.
> 
> ## 7) What the ingest side should look like next
> 
> Your blueprint is very clear here:
> - file enters local inbox
> - register in `qiarchive`
> - assign canonical ID and short code
> - extract
> - chunk
> - embed locally
> - push vectors to pgvector
> - optionally project into Neo4j
> - never let graph/vector become canonical truth fileciteturn2file1
> 
> So the next build should be:
> 
> **Local Python service**
> - `/srv/qios/python_local/api/main.py`
> - `/srv/qios/python_local/file_pipeline/watch.py`
> - `/srv/qios/python_local/services/extract.py`
> - `/srv/qios/python_local/services/embed.py`
> - `/srv/qios/python_local/jobs/project_graph.py`
> 
> **The worker logic**
> 1. watch `/srv/qidata/inbox`
> 2. checksum + register file in Supabase
> 3. extract text
> 4. chunk deterministically
> 5. call Ollama `/api/embed`
> 6. write embeddings to Supabase `vector` columns
> 7. project document/chunk/entity graph into Neo4j
> 
> Supabase’s pgvector extension is `vector`, and it’s the right place for canonical retrieval-side embeddings in your architecture. Neo4j vector indexes should declare the embedding dimension explicitly so dimension mismatches fail fast. citeturn775328view9turn839110search3
> 
> A Neo4j vector index will look like:
> 
> ```cypher
> CREATE VECTOR INDEX chunk_embedding IF NOT EXISTS
> FOR (c:Chunk)
> ON c.embedding
> OPTIONS {indexConfig: {
>   `vector.dimensions`: 768,
>   `vector.similarity_function`: 'cosine'
> }};
> ```
> 
> Use the actual dimension for the embedding model you choose. citeturn839110search3
> 
> ## The important boundary
> 
> Do **not** do these yet:
> - no public Cloudflare tunnel to raw Ollama
> - no public Neo4j
> - no Redis exposed outside Docker
> - no second vector store “just because”
> 
> For tonight, the winning state is:
> 
> - Ubuntu server reachable by Tailscale
> - Docker working
> - Ollama running
> - Open WebUI reachable privately over Tailscale
> - Neo4j up
> - `/srv/qidata` created
> - ready for local Python ingest worker
> 
> That gets you from “box on the network” to “actual private AI node.”
> 
> The next best step is to build the first worker: **watch inbox → register → extract → chunk → embed → upsert to Supabase → project to Neo4j**.

### Assistant
> Yep. You’ve got the base in place.
> 
> Right now your state is:
> 
> - **Tailscale is up**
> - **Docker is up**
> - **your runtime folders exist**
> - **the missing pieces are Ollama, Open WebUI, and private access to the UI**
> 
> Also, that Tailscale message about SSH ACLs is **not** a blocker for normal SSH over the Tailscale IP. It just means **Tailscale SSH** itself is not currently allowed by your tailnet policy. Tailscale SSH requires matching access-control policy on top of enabling `--ssh`. citeturn584612search2turn584612search3
> 
> This next sequence is the one I want you to run.
> 
> This also matches the local-runtime direction in your uploaded QiOS blueprint. fileciteturn0file0
> 
> ## Do this from where you are now
> 
> ### 0. Sanity checks first
> 
> Run these:
> 
> ```bash
> whoami
> hostname
> docker --version
> docker compose version
> tailscale ip
> tailscale status
> ```
> 
> If `docker compose version` fails, stop there and tell me exactly what it prints.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 1. Install Ollama on the host
> 
> Ollama’s official Linux install is the shell script below, and on Linux it runs as a `systemd` service. The docs also show `systemctl start ollama`, `systemctl status ollama`, and `journalctl -e -u ollama` for service management. citeturn769061view3turn769061view0
> 
> Run:
> 
> ```bash
> curl -fsSL https://ollama.com/install.sh | sh
> sudo systemctl start ollama
> sudo systemctl enable ollama
> sudo systemctl status ollama --no-pager
> ```
> 
> Then verify the binary:
> 
> ```bash
> ollama -v
> ```
> 
> If the service fails, inspect logs:
> 
> ```bash
> journalctl -e -u ollama --no-pager
> ```
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 2. Let Docker containers talk to Ollama
> 
> By default, Ollama binds to `127.0.0.1:11434`. Ollama’s docs say to change that with `OLLAMA_HOST`, and on Linux the supported way is `systemctl edit ollama.service`, then reload and restart. citeturn769061view2turn769061view5
> 
> Instead of making you edit manually, use this exact override file:
> 
> ```bash
> sudo mkdir -p /etc/systemd/system/ollama.service.d
> 
> sudo tee /etc/systemd/system/ollama.service.d/override.conf > /dev/null <<'EOF'
> [Service]
> Environment="OLLAMA_HOST=0.0.0.0:11434"
> Environment="OLLAMA_NO_CLOUD=1"
> EOF
> ```
> 
> Then reload:
> 
> ```bash
> sudo systemctl daemon-reload
> sudo systemctl restart ollama
> sudo systemctl status ollama --no-pager
> ```
> 
> Why `OLLAMA_NO_CLOUD=1`? Ollama documents that this disables its cloud features and keeps it local-only. citeturn769061view2
> 
> Now verify the port is listening:
> 
> ```bash
> ss -tulpn | grep 11434
> ```
> 
> You should see Ollama on `0.0.0.0:11434`.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 3. Pull one chat model and one embedding model
> 
> You do **not** need ten models right now.
> 
> Start with:
> - one chat model
> - one embedding model
> 
> Run:
> 
> ```bash
> ollama pull llama3.2
> ollama pull embeddinggemma
> ```
> 
> Test generation:
> 
> ```bash
> curl http://127.0.0.1:11434/api/generate -d '{
>   "model": "llama3.2",
>   "prompt": "Say hello from qiserver in one sentence.",
>   "stream": false
> }'
> ```
> 
> Test embeddings:
> 
> ```bash
> curl http://127.0.0.1:11434/api/embed -d '{
>   "model": "embeddinggemma",
>   "input": "This is a test embedding from qiserver."
> }'
> ```
> 
> Ollama documents the local server config and bind behavior in the FAQ, including that it binds to `127.0.0.1:11434` by default unless changed with `OLLAMA_HOST`. citeturn769061view2turn769061view5
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 4. Create the Docker app folders for the UI and graph
> 
> Run:
> 
> ```bash
> mkdir -p /srv/qios/open-webui
> mkdir -p /srv/qios/neo4j/{data,logs,plugins}
> mkdir -p /srv/qios/compose
> ```
> 
> Generate a secret key:
> 
> ```bash
> openssl rand -hex 32
> ```
> 
> Copy that output.
> 
> Now create the env file:
> 
> ```bash
> cat > /srv/qios/compose/.env <<'EOF'
> WEBUI_SECRET_KEY=PASTE_REAL_SECRET_HERE
> NEO4J_PASSWORD=ChangeThisNeo4jNow123!
> EOF
> ```
> 
> Then edit it once to replace the placeholder:
> 
> ```bash
> nano /srv/qios/compose/.env
> ```
> 
> Open WebUI explicitly recommends setting `WEBUI_SECRET_KEY` so you don’t get logged out every time the container is recreated. Docker is the officially recommended install path for most users. citeturn531169view1
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 5. Create the Docker Compose file
> 
> Open WebUI’s docs recommend Docker for most users, note that WebSocket support is required, and show both simple `docker run` and Compose examples. citeturn531169view1
> 
> Create this file:
> 
> ```bash
> cat > /srv/qios/compose/docker-compose.yml <<'EOF'
> services:
>   openwebui:
>     image: ghcr.io/open-webui/open-webui:main
>     container_name: open-webui
>     restart: unless-stopped
>     ports:
>       - "127.0.0.1:3000:8080"
>     environment:
>       WEBUI_SECRET_KEY: ${WEBUI_SECRET_KEY}
>       OLLAMA_BASE_URL: http://host.docker.internal:11434
>     extra_hosts:
>       - "host.docker.internal:host-gateway"
>     volumes:
>       - /srv/qios/open-webui:/app/backend/data
> 
>   neo4j:
>     image: neo4j:latest
>     container_name: neo4j
>     restart: unless-stopped
>     ports:
>       - "127.0.0.1:7474:7474"
>       - "127.0.0.1:7687:7687"
>     environment:
>       NEO4J_AUTH: neo4j/${NEO4J_PASSWORD}
>     volumes:
>       - /srv/qios/neo4j/data:/data
>       - /srv/qios/neo4j/logs:/logs
>       - /srv/qios/neo4j/plugins:/plugins
> EOF
> ```
> 
> Important details in that file:
> 
> - `127.0.0.1:3000:8080` keeps Open WebUI local-only
> - `127.0.0.1:7474` and `127.0.0.1:7687` keep Neo4j local-only
> - `host.docker.internal:host-gateway` gives the container a way to reach Ollama running on the host
> 
> That last Linux detail matters because you are running Ollama on the host, not inside Docker.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 6. Start the containers
> 
> Run:
> 
> ```bash
> cd /srv/qios/compose
> docker compose pull
> docker compose up -d
> docker ps
> ```
> 
> Then inspect logs:
> 
> ```bash
> docker logs open-webui --tail 100
> docker logs neo4j --tail 100
> ```
> 
> If Open WebUI is healthy, test from the server itself:
> 
> ```bash
> curl -I http://127.0.0.1:3000
> ```
> 
> If you want a cleaner test:
> 
> ```bash
> curl http://127.0.0.1:3000 | head
> ```
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 7. Make the UI reachable privately over Tailscale
> 
> Tailscale Serve is the right move here, not Funnel. Serve is private to your tailnet, requires HTTPS enabled in the tailnet, and Tailscale explicitly says it is best practice for the backend service to listen only on localhost. citeturn110444view1turn110444view0turn110444view4
> 
> First do an interactive test:
> 
> ```bash
> tailscale serve 3000
> ```
> 
> That should give you a URL under your tailnet domain, something like:
> 
> ```text
> https://qiserver-1.cerberus-sirius.ts.net
> ```
> 
> If it asks you to enable HTTPS certificates for the tailnet, approve it in the browser flow. Tailscale documents that `tailscale serve` will prompt you if HTTPS isn’t enabled yet. citeturn110444view1
> 
> Once it works, make it persistent across reboots with `--bg`. Tailscale’s CLI docs explicitly say `--bg` keeps Serve persistent and it resumes after reboot or `tailscale down/up`. citeturn323134search1
> 
> Run:
> 
> ```bash
> tailscale serve --bg 3000
> tailscale serve status
> ```
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 8. What you should be able to do after that
> 
> From another device on your tailnet:
> 
> - open the Tailscale Serve URL
> - load Open WebUI
> - sign in
> - add Ollama as the backend if it doesn’t auto-detect it
> - chat with your local model
> 
> At that point, the box is doing the useful part:
> - local chat
> - private access
> - host compute off your main machine
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 9. Neo4j is up, but don’t overbuild it tonight
> 
> Neo4j can support vector indexes, but your canonical retrieval layer still belongs in Supabase `vector`, which is exactly what your blueprint says: vector retrieval in Postgres/pgvector, graph derived and non-canonical. Supabase’s docs confirm the `pgvector` extension name is `vector`, and that it is used to store embeddings for RAG. citeturn744119view0
> 
> So tonight:
> - **get Neo4j running**
> - **do not** start schema-design cosplay yet
> - **do not** start shoving random chunks into graph nodes without the pipeline contract
> 
> You want:
> 1. local AI node working
> 2. local UI working
> 3. private access working
> 4. then ingestion worker
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 10. Two small cleanup items
> 
> ### A. If Docker complains about permissions
> Docker’s docs note that if you previously ran Docker commands with `sudo`, your `~/.docker` directory can get wrong ownership. Fix with: citeturn288036view1
> 
> ```bash
> sudo chown "$USER":"$USER" /home/"$USER"/.docker -R
> sudo chmod g+rwx "$HOME/.docker" -R
> ```
> 
> ### B. The `docker` group is root-equivalent
> Docker’s docs explicitly warn that membership in the `docker` group grants root-level privileges. That’s fine for your admin user, just don’t casually add others. citeturn288036view0
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 11. The exact order I want you to follow now
> 
> Run in this order:
> 
> 1. install Ollama
> 2. set the systemd override
> 3. restart Ollama
> 4. pull `llama3.2`
> 5. pull `embeddinggemma`
> 6. test both API calls
> 7. create `.env`
> 8. create `docker-compose.yml`
> 9. `docker compose up -d`
> 10. test `http://127.0.0.1:3000`
> 11. `tailscale serve 3000`
> 12. then `tailscale serve --bg 3000`
> 
> If any step fails, send me the exact output of the failed command and I’ll correct it without making you guess.
> 
> If you want, after you finish these, I’ll give you the next block: the first **Python ingest worker** for `/srv/qidata/inbox` that chunks docs, calls Ollama embeddings, and writes into Supabase + Neo4j.

> 
> What’s **working**:
> - Ollama is up and listening on `*:11434`
> - the models pulled successfully
> - Docker is up
> - Neo4j is up
> - Tailscale Serve is configured correctly and is persisting in the background with `--bg` citeturn659978search0turn659978search1
> 
> What’s **not working yet**:
> - Open WebUI is not ready to serve cleanly on port `3000`, which is why both `curl http://127.0.0.1:3000` and the Tailscale URL will fail or reset. Open WebUI’s normal Docker behavior is to expose the UI on port 3000 on the host, backed by port 8080 in the container. citeturn232424view0
> 
> And yes, your overall direction still matches the blueprint: local runtime for ingest/embeddings/control plane, derived graph separate from canonical truth. fileciteturn0file0

> ## First: ignore these two things
> 
> These are **not problems**:
> 
> 1. `.docker` missing  
> That only matters if you previously created `~/.docker` with `sudo` and then got permission errors. If the directory does not exist, there is nothing to fix. Docker’s post-install docs only have you repair or remove `~/.docker` when that config-directory ownership problem actually exists. citeturn255547search0
> 
> 2. Tailscale Serve itself  
> Serve is fine. It is doing exactly what it should: proxying your tailnet URL to `http://127.0.0.1:3000`, and `--bg` makes it persist across reboot/restart. citeturn659978search0turn659978search1
> 
> ## The real issue
> 
> `open-webui` is still in `health: starting`, and your curl is getting `connection reset by peer`. That means the backend app inside the container is not fully ready yet, or it started and then hit a runtime problem after migrations.
> 
> The logs you pasted show a **ton** of DB migrations but not a clear “server ready” confirmation. That’s the giveaway.
> 
> ## Run these exact commands now
> 
> ### 1) Check whether Open WebUI ever becomes healthy
> 
> ```bash
> docker ps
> docker inspect --format='{{json .State.Health}}' open-webui
> docker inspect --format='{{.State.Status}} {{.State.Running}} {{.State.Restarting}} {{.State.ExitCode}}' open-webui
> ```
> 
> ### 2) Watch the live logs for 60 seconds
> 
> ```bash
> docker logs -f open-webui
> ```
> 
> Leave that running for a minute. You’re looking for one of these patterns:
> - it settles and starts serving
> - it throws a traceback/error
> - it keeps restarting
> - it hangs during startup
> 
> ### 3) Check whether the app is actually listening inside the container
> 
> ```bash
> docker exec -it open-webui sh -lc "ss -tulpn | grep 8080 || netstat -tulpn 2>/dev/null | grep 8080 || true"
> ```
> 
> ### 4) Test the app from inside the container
> 
> ```bash
> docker exec -it open-webui sh -lc "wget -qO- http://127.0.0.1:8080 | head -c 300"
> ```
> 
> If that works inside the container but not on the host, the port mapping is the issue.  
> If it fails inside the container too, the app itself is the issue.
> 
> ## Most likely fix if it’s stuck or unstable
> 
> Stop using the floating `:main` tag for now. Open WebUI’s own docs recommend pinning a specific version for production instead of using a floating tag like `:main`. citeturn232424view0
> 
> That matters because `:main` moves. You may have pulled a build with a migration or startup issue.
> 
> ### Edit your compose file
> 
> Open it:
> 
> ```bash
> nano /srv/qios/compose/docker-compose.yml
> ```
> 
> Change this:
> 
> ```yaml
> image: ghcr.io/open-webui/open-webui:main
> ```
> 
> to this:
> 
> ```yaml
> image: ghcr.io/open-webui/open-webui:v0.8.6
> ```
> 
> That version is explicitly listed in the current Open WebUI quick-start docs as a pin-able release example. citeturn232424view0
> 
> Then recreate only that container:
> 
> ```bash
> cd /srv/qios/compose
> docker compose down
> docker compose pull
> docker compose up -d
> docker ps
> docker logs -f open-webui
> ```
> 
> ## If you want the fastest “make it usable tonight” mode
> 
> You can also temporarily force single-user mode to reduce auth/setup friction. Open WebUI documents `WEBUI_AUTH=False` for single-user mode, with the warning that you should not switch back and forth casually. citeturn232424view0
> 
> Only do this if you want a quick private box for yourself.
> 
> Add under `environment:` in the Open WebUI service:
> 
> ```yaml
>       WEBUI_AUTH: "False"
> ```
> 
> Then recreate the container.
> 
> Since this is already private behind Tailscale Serve, that is acceptable for a personal node tonight. The tradeoff is that you are opting into single-user mode behavior documented by Open WebUI. citeturn232424view0
>

> ```
> 
> That one is straight from your terminal output, so no mystery there.

### Assistant
> Perfect. It’s live.
> 
> What your output proves:
> - Open WebUI is serving on `127.0.0.1:3000`
> - Ollama is reachable and exposing both models
> - the Tailscale URL is working
> - the missing `wget` inside the container does **not** matter
> 
> So stage 1 is done: **private AI node online**.
> 
> Two important corrections now.
> 
> First: **do not leave `embeddinggemma` as your chat default**.  
> That model is for embeddings, semantic search, retrieval, and RAG, not normal conversation. Ollama’s docs explicitly list `embeddinggemma` under embeddings and show it being used through `/api/embed`. Open WebUI’s RAG docs also make clear the embedding model is part of document indexing/re-indexing, which is a different role from your chat model. citeturn959410search0turn788483search4
> 
> Use:
> - **`llama3.2`** for chat
> - **`embeddinggemma`** for vectorization / RAG
> 
> Second: that **“Not secure”** browser badge is the only obvious cleanup item left.  
> Tailscale Serve requires HTTPS certificates to be enabled for the tailnet, and the admin console can report machine certificate states including **“No certificate found.”** Your earlier machine details already showed **No certificate found**, so the browser warning is very likely tied to that. Tailscale’s HTTPS docs say to enable **MagicDNS** and **HTTPS Certificates** in the admin DNS settings, then check the machine’s TLS certificate status again. citeturn788483search0turn788483search1
> 
> So the move now is:
> 
> 1. In Open WebUI, set **`llama3.2:latest`** as the default chat model.  
> 2. In Tailscale admin, check:
>    - **MagicDNS enabled**
>    - **HTTPS certificates enabled**
>    - machine TLS status changes from **No certificate found** to **Valid** citeturn788483search1
> 3. Leave `embeddinggemma` installed, but treat it as pipeline infrastructure, not your assistant.
> 
> Also, do **not** let Open WebUI become your canonical knowledge system. Your blueprint is explicit that vector and graph are derived layers, not truth sources, and that the local runtime should handle ingest, extraction, chunking, embeddings, and local API control before downstream retrieval/projection. fileciteturn0file0
> 
> So the clean architecture now is:
> 
> - **Open WebUI** = private chat/admin surface
> - **Ollama** = local model runtime
> - **Neo4j** = derived graph
> - **your future worker** = real ingest/chunk/embed/project pipeline
> - **Supabase/pgvector** = canonical retrieval side, not Open WebUI’s internal file store fileciteturn0file0
> 
> Your next real step is **not** more UI tweaking. It is:
> 
> **build the first ingest worker on the server**
> 
> It should do this:
> 
> - watch `/srv/qidata/inbox`
> - extract text
> - chunk deterministically
> - call Ollama `/api/embed`
> - upsert chunks + embeddings to Supabase
> - optionally project document/entity relationships into Neo4j
> 
> That is the piece that turns the server from “private chatbot box” into “actual QiOS compute node.”

> ## 2) Create one status command
> 
> Run this:
> 
> ```bash
> sudo tee /usr/local/bin/qiserver-status > /dev/null <<'EOF'
> #!/usr/bin/env bash
> set -e
> 
> echo "=============================="
> echo "QISERVER STATUS"
> echo "=============================="
> echo "Host: $(hostname)"
> echo "User: $(whoami)"
> echo
> 
> echo "--- Tailscale ---"
> tailscale ip || true
> tailscale serve status || true
> echo
> 
> echo "--- Ollama ---"
> systemctl is-active ollama || true
> curl -s http://127.0.0.1:11434/api/tags || echo "Ollama API not responding"
> echo
> echo
> 
> echo "--- Docker ---"
> docker ps --format 'table {{.Names}}\t{{.Status}}\t{{.Ports}}'
> echo
> 
> echo "--- URLs ---"
> echo "Open WebUI: https://qiserver-1.cerberus-sirius.ts.net"
> echo "Local WebUI: http://127.0.0.1:3000"
> echo "Local Ollama: http://127.0.0.1:11434"
> echo "Neo4j: http://127.0.0.1:7474"
> echo
> 
> echo "--- Paths ---"
> echo "/srv/qios"
> echo "/srv/qios/compose"
> echo "/srv/qios/docs/000_RUN_ME_FIRST.md"
> echo "/srv/qidata"
> echo
> 
> echo "--- Next Step ---"
> echo "Read: /srv/qios/docs/000_RUN_ME_FIRST.md"
> EOF
> 
> sudo chmod +x /usr/local/bin/qiserver-status
> ```
> 
> Test it:
> 
> ```bash
> bash /usr/local/bin/qiserver-status
> ```

> ## 3) Add shell shortcuts and login reminder
> 
> Run this:
> 
> ```bash
> cat >> ~/.bashrc <<'EOF'
> 
> alias qs='bash /usr/local/bin/qiserver-status'
> alias qrunbook='nano /srv/qios/docs/000_RUN_ME_FIRST.md'
> alias qcompose='cd /srv/qios/compose'
> alias qdocs='cd /srv/qios/docs'
> alias qdata='cd /srv/qidata'
> 
> if [[ $- == *i* ]]; then
>   echo
>   echo "QISERVER → Runbook: /srv/qios/docs/000_RUN_ME_FIRST.md"
>   echo "QISERVER → Status: qs"
>   echo "QISERVER → UI: https://qiserver-1.cerberus-sirius.ts.net"
>   echo
> fi
> EOF
> 
> source ~/.bashrc
> ```
> 
> Now every time you SSH in, the server itself will tell you:
> - where the runbook is
> - how to check status
> - where the UI lives
> 
> That’s how you stop future-you from acting like this whole thing never existed.

> # QIServer Private AI Node
> 
> ## Purpose
> 
> QIServer is the current machine-local QiOS compute node for private AI access, local model execution, and future ingestion/runtime work. It exists to offload AI and document-processing workload from the primary workstation and to serve as the first live QiOS local runtime host.
> 
> ## Why This Node Exists
> 
> This node is the practical execution host for the local-runtime side of QiOS:
> 
> - private AI chat access
> - local model hosting
> - future ingestion and extraction
> - future chunking and embeddings
> - future local control-plane endpoints
> - future graph projection support
> 
> It is not the canonical source of truth for records. Canonical truth remains in the cloud-side data model. Derived layers remain downstream.
> 
> ## Current Role
> 
> ### Active now
> 
> - Tailscale-connected private server
> - Ollama running on host
> - Open WebUI running in Docker
> - Neo4j running in Docker
> - Tailscale Serve publishing Open WebUI privately to the tailnet
> 
> ### Planned next
> 
> - local Python API
> - inbox watcher
> - extraction pipeline
> - deterministic chunking
> - embedding worker
> - Supabase/pgvector upsert
> - Neo4j projection
> - queue/retry/status endpoints
> 
> ## Runtime Position in QiOS
> 
> QIServer implements the **local runtime** side of QiOS, not the canonical cloud data layer.
> 
> ### Local runtime responsibilities
> 
> - file watcher
> - ingest pipeline
> - OCR
> - extraction
> - chunking
> - embeddings
> - local API
> - machine-local state
> 
> ### Cloud-side responsibilities
> 
> - canonical metadata
> - retrieval index in pgvector
> - app-facing APIs
> - review surfaces
> - tenant-aware data serving
> 
> ### Boundary rule
> 
> Local runtime writes registrations, metadata, and embeddings outward.
> Cloud runtime serves application surfaces.
> Graph, vector, and AI layers do not become canonical truth.
>

> 
> ### Private access surface

> - Open WebUI: `https://qiserver-1.cerberus-sirius.ts.net`

### Assistant
> Yes. I’d change both.
> 
> Your **original placement idea was close**, but given the **actual docs tree**, the cleanest placement is:
> 
> ```text
> docs/05_compute/40_runtime/qiserver_runtime_profile.md
> ```
> 
> Why that is the right home:
> 
> - `05_compute` already contains the compute-layer buckets: APIs, workers, pipelines, runtime, and integrations fileciteturn9file0
> - inside that, `40_runtime` already holds `runtime.md` and `tech_stack.md`, so a **machine-specific runtime profile** belongs there, not loose at the top of `05_compute` fileciteturn9file0
> - the blueprint defines the **local runtime** as file watcher, ingest pipeline, OCR, extraction, chunking, embeddings, local API, and local state, with graph/vector remaining derived downstream layers fileciteturn9file2
> 
> So the placement is:
> 
> **Correct placement**
> ```text
> docs/05_compute/40_runtime/qiserver_runtime_profile.md
> ```
> 
> Not:
> - `docs/05_compute/0510...` — wrong for the actual file tree you showed
> - `adr/` — wrong unless you’re locking a new architectural decision
> - `06_applications/` — wrong, because this is infrastructure/runtime, not UI surface
> - `08_appendices/` — wrong, because this is live operational architecture, not reference fluff
> 
> ## Title
> 
> I would also tighten the title.
> 
> **Do not use** “QIServer Private AI Node” as the main title.  
> That title is too narrow now. This box is already more than a chatbot host. It is becoming the local runtime node for:
> - Ollama
> - Open WebUI
> - Neo4j
> - future ingest
> - future chunk/embed/project workers
> 
> So the better title is:
> 
> **`QIServer Runtime Profile`**
> 
> That gives you:
> - broad enough scope
> - matches the `40_runtime` section
> - still specific to this host
> - won’t age badly once you add ingestion and workers
> 
> ## Final recommendation
> 
> **Path**
> ```text
> docs/05_compute/40_runtime/qiserver_runtime_profile.md
> ```
> 
> **Document title**
> ```markdown
> # QIServer Runtime Profile
> ```
> 
> ## Filename alternatives if you want a slightly stronger emphasis
> 
> Best:
> - `qiserver_runtime_profile.md`
> 
> Acceptable:
> - `qiserver_local_runtime_node.md`
> 
> Less good:
> - `qiserver_private_ai_node.md`  
>   Too narrow.
> - `qiserver_control_plane.md`  
>   Too specific; that’s only part of what it is.
> - `qiserver_ollama_server.md`  
>   Way too narrow and future-fragile.
> 
> ## ADR question
> 
> You **do not** need a new ADR just for this document placement.
> 
> But if you decide the pattern is now formalized as:
> - backup/secondary machine as QiOS local runtime host
> - Tailscale private edge access
> - host Ollama + containerized services + derived graph
> - local compute node as a standard deployment pattern
> 
> Then that belongs in an ADR update or new ADR. And you already have `ADR-0009_backup_server_control_plane_and_public_edge_pattern.md` in the repo, which sounds like the closest existing home for the **architectural pattern**, not this runtime profile doc itself fileciteturn9file0
> 
> So the split is:
> 
> - **runtime profile doc** → `docs/05_compute/40_runtime/qiserver_runtime_profile.md`
> - **architecture decision about this becoming a repeatable pattern** → maybe update `ADR-0009`
>

### Assistant
> What you have open now is **not** a server admin UI. It’s **Open WebUI**.
> 
> That’s why you only see:
> - Ollama models
> - chats
> - knowledge/doc features
> 
> Open WebUI is an AI interface for conversations, files, tools, and knowledge retrieval. It is **not** a Docker/container manager. Its docs describe it as one interface for models, conversations, files, tools, and knowledge bases, and its Knowledge feature is for AI-searchable documents/RAG, not infrastructure management. citeturn279091search3turn279091search6
> 
> So the answer is:
> 
> ## 1) No, you do not “convert” Open WebUI into the server admin UI
> 
> That’s the wrong mental model.
> 
> You need **separate surfaces**:
> 
> - **Open WebUI** → AI chat / model use / document knowledge
> - **Docker admin UI** → manage containers, images, stacks, volumes
> - **QiOS local admin control plane** → your future custom queue/ingest/runtime UI
> - **DB/admin surface** → later for canonical data ops
> 
> Your own blueprint already separates these responsibilities. It defines a **Local Admin Control Plane** as a standalone React/Vite admin portal plus FastAPI local control server for queue, ingest, retry, and system status, and it also separates **Admin** from **Portal**. It also says NocoDB is the admin surface over Postgres, while local Python handles file processing, OCR, and embeddings. fileciteturn10file2 fileciteturn10file3 fileciteturn10file4
> 
> So:  
> **Open WebUI is not the admin plane.**  
> It should stay the AI/chat surface.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 2) How you should work with Docker apps on this server
> 
> Use this structure:
> 
> ```text
> /srv/qios/stacks/
>   open-webui/
>   neo4j/
>   portainer/
>   n8n/
>   paperless/
>   whatever-next/
> ```
> 
> Right now you have everything in `/srv/qios/compose`, which is fine for the first night, but it will get messy fast.
> 
> Better pattern:
> 
> - one folder per app/stack
> - one `docker-compose.yml` per stack
> - one `.env` per stack if needed
> - bind mounts or volumes for persistent data
> 
> Docker’s docs say:
> - **volumes** are the preferred mechanism for persistent data managed by Docker
> - **bind mounts** are appropriate when you want files/config/data visible directly on the host filesystem
> - Compose supports both via the `volumes` attribute. citeturn279091search2turn279091search0turn279091search5
> 
> ### Practical rule
> Use:
> - **bind mounts** when you want to directly inspect/edit files on the server
> - **named Docker volumes** when you just want persistence and cleaner Docker-managed storage
> 
> For your style, bind mounts are often better because you like visible structure.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 3) The install pattern for any new Docker app
> 
> This is the pattern you should follow every time:
> 
> ### Step A — make a stack folder
> Example:
> 
> ```bash
> mkdir -p /srv/qios/stacks/portainer
> cd /srv/qios/stacks/portainer
> ```
> 
> ### Step B — create `docker-compose.yml`
> Put the app definition there.
> 
> ### Step C — create any data folders or `.env`
> If the app needs persistent config/data, make those too.
> 
> ### Step D — bring it up
> ```bash
> docker compose up -d
> ```
> 
> ### Step E — check it
> ```bash
> docker ps
> docker logs <container-name> --tail 100
> ```
> 
> ### Step F — decide exposure
> - local only
> - Tailscale only
> - later maybe Cloudflare/private edge
> 
> That’s the repeatable flow.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 4) What you should install for Docker GUI admin
> 
> Install **Portainer**.
> 
> Portainer’s official docs describe it as a UI for building and managing containers and environments, and its Environments docs show that it manages Docker environments and lets you add/manage them from the dashboard. citeturn279091search7turn279091search1turn279091search4
> 
> That is the missing GUI you are actually asking for.
> 
> ### Why Portainer fits
> Because it gives you a GUI for:
> - containers
> - images
> - volumes
> - networks
> - stacks / compose
> - environment-level management
> 
> That is what Open WebUI does **not** do.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 5) Clean architecture for your server UIs
> 
> Use this split:
> 
> - **Open WebUI** → `ai.qiserver` in your head  
>   AI chat and knowledge work only
> - **Portainer** → `ops.qiserver` in your head  
>   Docker/container admin GUI
> - **future QiOS Local Admin Control Plane**  
>   your custom ingest/queue/retry/status interface
> - **later NocoDB/admin over Supabase**  
>   canonical record ops, not machine ops
> 
> That split matches your blueprint way better than trying to jam everything into one UI. Your blueprint explicitly separates machine-local control, admin surface, and user-facing portal concerns. fileciteturn10file2 fileciteturn10file4
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 6) The reason you “don’t see what we installed”
> 
> Because Open WebUI only knows about:
> - the Ollama models it can use
> - its own chats
> - its own knowledge/workspace features
> 
> It does **not** inspect Docker and show you “hey here’s Neo4j, here’s Portainer, here’s paperless.” That is outside its product scope. Open WebUI’s docs describe knowledge, workspaces, model chats, tools, and retrieval features — not infrastructure/container management. citeturn279091search3turn279091search6
> 
> So nothing is missing. You were just looking in the wrong window.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 7) My recommendation: do this next
> 
> ### Install Portainer as its own stack
> 
> Use this path:
> 
> ```text
> /srv/qios/stacks/portainer/
> ```
> 
> Then later expose it privately the same way you exposed Open WebUI.
> 
> ### Keep Open WebUI exactly what it is
> Do not try to make it your ops dashboard.
> 
> ### Build your future custom server UI separately
> That future UI should reflect your blueprint:
> - queue
> - status
> - ingest trigger
> - retry
> - logs
> - worker health
> - maybe model status
> 
> That is your **Local Admin Control Plane**, not Open WebUI. Your blueprint already defines it that way. fileciteturn10file2
>

> 
> ## 8) Blunt truth
> 
> You are trying to make one screen do four jobs:
> - chat UI
> - model manager
> - container admin
> - pipeline control plane
> 
> That’s how systems turn into mush.
> 
> Don’t do that.
>

> - **Portainer** for Docker admin
> - **Open WebUI** for AI use

>

> 
> When you ask, “Where do I manage this?”
> 
> Use this:
> 
> - **Is it a model/chat/doc thing?** → Open WebUI
> - **Is it a container/app thing?** → Portainer
