### User
> no i mean im logged into the actual server that has ubuntu desktop.

### Assistant
> Ah. Then the issue is simpler.
> 
> You’re mixing up **the Ubuntu desktop** with **the apps running on the server**.
> 
> Those Docker services are **not native Ubuntu apps**. They do **not** show up in the app launcher like normal installed programs. They are **web apps/services** running on the machine.
> 
> So on the actual Ubuntu desktop:
> 
> - **Open WebUI** is at `http://127.0.0.1:3000`
> - **Neo4j Browser** is at `http://127.0.0.1:7474`
> - **Ollama** is a backend service at `http://127.0.0.1:11434` and has **no built-in desktop GUI**
> 
> So the way you “use” what you installed is:
> 
> 1. log into Ubuntu desktop
> 2. open Firefox or Chrome
> 3. go to those local addresses
> 
> That’s it.
> 
> ## What each thing is
> 
> **Ubuntu desktop**
> - the host OS
> - file manager, terminal, browser, settings
> 
> **Docker containers**
> - background services/apps
> - usually accessed by browser or API
> - not automatically shown in the Ubuntu app menu
> 
> **Open WebUI**
> - your AI chat front end
> 
> **Neo4j**
> - graph database with its own browser UI
> 
> **Ollama**
> - model runtime/API, not a normal GUI app
> 
> ## What you actually want for “admin stuff using the GUI”
> 
> You want **two different admin layers**:
> 
> ### 1. Host admin GUI
> For the actual Ubuntu machine itself:
> - services
> - logs
> - storage
> - users
> - updates
> 
> Install **Cockpit** for that.
> 
> Run:
> 
> ```bash
> sudo apt update
> sudo apt install -y cockpit
> sudo systemctl enable --now cockpit.socket
> ```
> 
> Then open on the Ubuntu desktop:
> 
> ```text
> https://127.0.0.1:9090
> ```
> 
> Log in with your normal Linux username and password.
> 
> That gives you a real GUI for the machine itself.
> 
> ### 2. Docker admin GUI
> For containers, images, volumes, networks, stacks:
> - install **Portainer**
> 
> That is separate from Cockpit.
> 
> So the clean split is:
> 
> - **Cockpit** = Ubuntu host admin
> - **Portainer** = Docker admin
> - **Open WebUI** = AI/chat UI
> 
> That’s the correct mental model.
> 
> ## Why you don’t “see” the apps
> 
> Because they are not installed like:
> - LibreOffice
> - Files
> - Settings
> - VS Code
> 
> They are running as services and exposed on ports.
> 
> So the Ubuntu desktop is just the place from which you open the browser and reach:
> - `127.0.0.1:3000`
> - `127.0.0.1:7474`
> - later `127.0.0.1:9090`
> - later Portainer too
> 
> ## Best immediate move
> 
> On the Ubuntu desktop, open a browser and bookmark these:
> 
> - `http://127.0.0.1:3000` → **QiServer AI**
> - `http://127.0.0.1:7474` → **QiServer Neo4j**
> - `https://127.0.0.1:9090` → **QiServer Host Admin** after Cockpit install
> 
> ## Even better: make them show up like apps
> 
> Create desktop launchers so they behave like clickable apps.
> 
> Run this on the Ubuntu machine:
> 
> ```bash
> mkdir -p ~/.local/share/applications
> 
> cat > ~/.local/share/applications/qiserver-ai.desktop <<'EOF'
> [Desktop Entry]
> Version=1.0
> Type=Application
> Name=QiServer AI
> Exec=xdg-open http://127.0.0.1:3000
> Icon=web-browser
> Terminal=false
> Categories=Network;
> EOF
> 
> cat > ~/.local/share/applications/qiserver-neo4j.desktop <<'EOF'
> [Desktop Entry]
> Version=1.0
> Type=Application
> Name=QiServer Neo4j
> Exec=xdg-open http://127.0.0.1:7474
> Icon=web-browser
> Terminal=false
> Categories=Network;
> EOF
> ```
> 
> After Cockpit is installed:
> 
> ```bash
> cat > ~/.local/share/applications/qiserver-admin.desktop <<'EOF'
> [Desktop Entry]
> Version=1.0
> Type=Application
> Name=QiServer Admin
> Exec=xdg-open https://127.0.0.1:9090
> Icon=web-browser
> Terminal=false
> Categories=System;
> EOF
> ```
> 
> Then they should appear in the app launcher like normal clickable entries.
> 
> ## The blunt answer
> 
> No, the current AI UI is not your server UI.  
> No, Ubuntu desktop will not magically show Docker apps as installed desktop software.  
> Yes, you can access them locally in the browser.  
> And yes, **Cockpit** is the missing piece for host admin GUI.
> 
> Next clean step:
> - install Cockpit now
> - then install Portainer after that so you have both host GUI and Docker GUI

### User
> umm i need to ssh into the server first no.

### Assistant
> No.
> 
> **If you are sitting at the Ubuntu desktop itself, you do not need SSH first.**  
> You can just open the browser on that machine and go to:
> 
> - `http://127.0.0.1:3000` → Open WebUI
> - `http://127.0.0.1:7474` → Neo4j
> - later `https://127.0.0.1:9090` → Cockpit
> 
> You only need **SSH** when:
> 
> - you are connecting **from another machine** to manage the server by terminal
> - you want to run commands, edit files, restart services, install stuff
> 
> So the split is:
> 
> - **Use the server locally:** open browser, no SSH needed
> - **Manage the server remotely:** SSH needed
> 
> If you are on your other PC and want to remote-manage it, then yes, SSH into the Tailscale IP or hostname first, like:
> 
> ```bash
> ssh qiadmin@100.121.111.106
> ```
> 
> or if name resolution is working:
> 
> ```bash
> ssh qiadmin@qiserver-1
> ```
> 
> If you’re on the Ubuntu desktop right now, just open Firefox and type `http://127.0.0.1:3000`.

### User
> but i want to tusd my main desktop not the makshift cheap keyboard in my lap to do stuff wtith the server.

### Assistant
> Yes — **if you want to control the server from your main desktop, use SSH for terminal/admin work**.
> 
> Two important distinctions:
> 
> - **SSH = terminal control**
> - **not** the Ubuntu desktop GUI
> 
> So from your main desktop, the normal move is:
> 
> ```bash
> ssh qiadmin@100.121.111.106
> ```
> 
> or, if MagicDNS/name resolution works on that machine:
> 
> ```bash
> ssh qiadmin@qiserver-1
> ```
> 
> Tailscale SSH is a special mode that takes over SSH on port 22 for Tailscale traffic, but it only works if your tailnet policy explicitly allows SSH access to that destination and user. Your earlier health check already warned that Tailscale SSH is enabled on the node but blocked by access controls, which matches Tailscale’s docs. citeturn972713search0turn972713search2
> 
> So the blunt version is:
> 
> - **For command-line management right now:** SSH from your main desktop.
> - **For the web apps we already installed:** just open them in your browser from your main desktop:
>   - `https://qiserver-1.cerberus-sirius.ts.net` → Open WebUI
> - **If you want the full Ubuntu desktop remotely:** that is **remote desktop**, not SSH.
> 
> So no, you do **not** need to sit at the cheap keyboard to manage the server.  
> You only need that if you want local physical access.
> 
> Your clean next move is:
> 1. SSH into the server from your main desktop
> 2. keep using the Tailscale URL for Open WebUI
> 3. later add a real remote desktop or Cockpit/Portainer, depending on whether you want host GUI or Docker GUI

### User
> something must of chagnes. Microsoft Windows [Version 10.0.29570.1000]

> (c) Microsoft Corporation. All rights reserved.

> 

> C:\Users\codyr>ssh qiadmin@100.121.111.106

> The authenticity of host '100.121.111.106 (100.121.111.106)' can't be established.

> ED25519 key fingerprint is SHA256:1d76etUaR1k4cPwyLs5BlHfdWUE781/WyWxR0G3eZ6g.

> This host key is known by the following other names/addresses:

>     C:\Users\codyr/.ssh/known_hosts:7: 192.168.0.78

> Are you sure you want to continue connecting (yes/no/[fingerprint])? yes

> Warning: Permanently added '100.121.111.106' (ED25519) to the list of known hosts.

> tailscale: tailnet policy does not permit you to SSH as user "qiadmin"

> Connection closed by 100.121.111.106 port 22

> 

> C:\Users\codyr>ssh qiadmin@100.121.111.106

> tailscale: tailnet policy does not permit you to SSH as user "qiadmin"

> Connection closed by 100.121.111.106 port 22

> 

> C:\Users\codyr>

### Assistant
> Yep. Something **did** change, and it was us.
> 
> You enabled **Tailscale SSH** on the server. When Tailscale SSH is enabled, Tailscale **takes over port 22 for connections arriving over the Tailscale network**. Your client is no longer hitting normal OpenSSH first; it is hitting Tailscale’s SSH layer, and your tailnet policy currently does **not** allow you to SSH in as `qiadmin`. citeturn167575search0turn807101search1
> 
> The host-key message is not the problem. It just means Windows sees the **same server key** under a new address (`100.121.111.106`) and already knew it as `192.168.0.78`. That part is normal.
> 
> ## Fastest fix
> 
> On the server, from the session you already have open, run:
> 
> ```bash
> sudo tailscale set --ssh=false
> ```
> 
> Tailscale documents `tailscale set --ssh=false` as the way to **disable Tailscale SSH on a device**. citeturn167575search0
> 
> Then from your main desktop, try again:
> 
> ```powershell
> ssh qiadmin@100.121.111.106
> ```
> 
> If normal OpenSSH is running on Ubuntu, that should drop you back to regular SSH over the tailnet IP.
> 
> ## If that still fails
> 
> Check that the standard SSH server is running on Ubuntu:
> 
> ```bash
> sudo systemctl status ssh
> ```
> 
> If needed:
> 
> ```bash
> sudo apt update
> sudo apt install -y openssh-server
> sudo systemctl enable --now ssh
> sudo systemctl status ssh
> ```
> 
> ## The other path, if you want to keep Tailscale SSH
> 
> Then you need to **change the tailnet policy file** so your user is allowed to SSH to this destination as `qiadmin`. Tailscale’s SSH access is controlled by the `ssh` section of the tailnet policy file, and rules can target destinations like `autogroup:self` or a device tag such as `tag:server`. The allowed OS usernames are set in the `users` field. citeturn807101search1turn807101search2
> 
> A minimal example for a tagged server would look like:
> 
> ```json
> "ssh": [
>   {
>     "action": "accept",
>     "src": ["your-email@example.com"],
>     "dst": ["tag:server"],
>     "users": ["qiadmin"]
>   }
> ]
> ```
> 
> Given where you are right now, that is **not** the path I’d use first. It is extra policy work for no benefit unless you specifically want Tailscale-managed SSH.
> 
> ## My recommendation
> 
> Do this:
> 
> 1. `sudo tailscale set --ssh=false` on the server
> 2. retry `ssh qiadmin@100.121.111.106` from Windows
> 3. only mess with Tailscale SSH policy later if you decide you actually want it
> 
> That gets you back to simple, boring, reliable SSH instead of ACL drama.
> 
> After you run `sudo tailscale set --ssh=false`, paste the result of:
> 
> ```bash
> sudo systemctl status ssh --no-pager
> ```
> 
> and then the next Windows SSH attempt.

### User
> ok im back in, now how do i get the cockpit and set up something that is cody proof wo we dont have this crisis again and also we need to get the server working, i have a stack of mail to opena nd scan, put to task, or bills, embed, ect.

### Assistant
> Good. Now stop trying to make one screen do everything.
> 
> You need **three surfaces** on this box:
> 
> - **Cockpit** = manage the Ubuntu host itself
> - **Portainer** = manage Docker containers/stacks
> - **Paperless-ngx** = chew through the mail pile with OCR and document intake
> 
> That split fits your blueprint better anyway: the local runtime is supposed to handle file watching, OCR, extraction, chunking, embeddings, and local API/control-plane work, while graph/vector stay derived and downstream. fileciteturn11file1 fileciteturn11file3
> 
> ## 1) Install Cockpit now
> 
> Cockpit’s Ubuntu install path is the backports package, and Cockpit is normally reached on port `9090`. It starts on demand via `cockpit.socket`, and you log in with your normal system account credentials. citeturn704629search0turn704629search6
> 
> Run this over SSH from your main desktop:
> 
> ```bash
> . /etc/os-release
> sudo apt update
> sudo apt install -t ${VERSION_CODENAME}-backports cockpit
> sudo systemctl enable --now cockpit.socket
> sudo systemctl status cockpit.socket --no-pager
> ```
> 
> Local access on the server itself:
> ```text
> https://127.0.0.1:9090
> ```
> 
> Remote access from your main desktop:
> ```text
> https://100.121.111.106:9090
> ```
> 
> If you want a cleaner Tailscale URL instead of remembering the port, use Tailscale Serve on a custom HTTPS port. Tailscale Serve supports custom HTTPS ports and reverse-proxy targets, including `https+insecure://127.0.0.1:9090` for a local TLS service. citeturn369779search0turn369779search1
> 
> Run:
> 
> ```bash
> sudo tailscale serve --https=9444 https+insecure://127.0.0.1:9090 --bg
> tailscale serve status
> ```
> 
> Then your Cockpit URL becomes:
> 
> ```text
> https://qiserver-1.cerberus-sirius.ts.net:9444
> ```
> 
> That is far more Cody-proof than “uhh what was the IP and port again.”
> 
> ## 2) Install Portainer next
> 
> Portainer is the Docker admin GUI. On Linux, their docs say the UI is on `9443`, and port `8000` is optional and only needed for Edge agents. citeturn328948view0
> 
> Make it private and simple:
> 
> ```bash
> sudo mkdir -p /srv/qios/stacks/portainer
> sudo chown -R $USER:$USER /srv/qios/stacks/portainer
> cd /srv/qios/stacks/portainer
> ```
> 
> Create the compose file:
> 
> ```bash
> cat > docker-compose.yml <<'EOF'
> services:
>   portainer:
>     image: portainer/portainer-ce:sts
>     container_name: portainer
>     restart: always
>     volumes:
>       - /var/run/docker.sock:/var/run/docker.sock
>       - portainer_data:/data
>     ports:
>       - "127.0.0.1:9443:9443"
> 
> volumes:
>   portainer_data:
>     name: portainer_data
> EOF
> ```
> 
> Bring it up:
> 
> ```bash
> docker compose up -d
> docker ps
> ```
> 
> Local URL on the server:
> ```text
> https://127.0.0.1:9443
> ```
> 
> And again, give yourself a clean Tailscale URL instead of raw ports. Serve supports custom HTTPS ports and reverse-proxy targets. citeturn369779search1
> 
> ```bash
> sudo tailscale serve --https=9445 https+insecure://127.0.0.1:9443 --bg
> tailscale serve status
> ```
> 
> Then Portainer becomes:
> 
> ```text
> https://qiserver-1.cerberus-sirius.ts.net:9445
> ```
> 
> Now you have:
> - `:9444` = host admin
> - `:9445` = Docker admin
> - root URL = AI chat
> 
> That is way less chaos.
> 
> ## 3) How to install future Docker apps without reinventing the wheel
> 
> Use one folder per stack:
> 
> ```text
> /srv/qios/stacks/
>   portainer/
>   paperless/
>   open-webui/
>   neo4j/
> ```
> 
> Rule:
> - create a stack folder
> - put `docker-compose.yml` there
> - `docker compose up -d`
> - add the URL to your runbook
> - add it to `qiserver-status`
> 
> That’s the install pattern. Stop doing mystery installs in random places.
> 
> ## 4) For the mail pile: install Paperless-ngx, not “embeddings first”
> 
> Blunt truth: **you do not need embeddings first. You need capture, OCR, and routing first.**
> 
> Paperless-ngx is the right intake layer for physical mail because its docs explicitly recommend Docker for most users, provide Docker Compose templates, support a **consume** directory bind mount, and expose the web app on `127.0.0.1:8000` by default after install. They also recommend PostgreSQL for new installations, though Docker defaults can still get you moving fast. citeturn328948view1turn314182view0
> 
> Paperless also has:
> - OCR settings
> - document consumption settings
> - optional email parsing
> - pre-consume and post-consume script hooks
> 
> Those last hooks matter because later we can trigger your Qi worker after Paperless ingests a document. citeturn314182view0
> 
> ### The right mail pipeline
> 
> Do this:
> 
> 1. **Open mail**
> 2. **Scan to Paperless consume folder**
> 3. **Paperless OCR + classify**
> 4. **You review and tag**:
>    - bill
>    - urgent
>    - tax
>    - legal
>    - action-needed
> 5. **Then** your Qi worker reads clean OCR text and metadata for embeddings / graph projection
>

### Assistant
> Yep. Two things happened.
> 
> First, **Cockpit is already installed and running**. Your log shows `cockpit.socket` went from inactive to active and port `9090` is now listening on the server. Cockpit’s normal access pattern is just `https://<ip-of-machine>:9090` with your normal system username and password. citeturn758311view2turn758311view3
> 
> Second, your `tailscale serve` command failed because the syntax was off. In current Tailscale, the command format is `tailscale serve [flags] <target>`, and the HTTPS form is `tailscale serve --https=<port> <target>`. The target can be a port, partial URL, or full URL. citeturn507397search0turn507397search2
> 
> The clean move right now is:
> 
> **Do not use Tailscale Serve for Cockpit yet.**
> You do not need it. Cockpit is already reachable directly over Tailscale on port 9090.
> 
> Open this from your main desktop:
> 
> ```text
> https://100.121.111.106:9090
> ```
> 
> or try:
> 
> ```text
> https://qiserver-1.cerberus-sirius.ts.net:9090
> ```
> 
> Log in with:
> - username: `qiadmin`
> - your normal Linux password
> 
> That is the shortest path and the least likely to create another detour. Cockpit is already there.
> 
> The other issue in your pasted log is that you pasted **prompt/output junk back into the shell**, so Bash started trying to execute garbage like `QISERVER:`, `tcp`, and fragments of old output as commands. That is why you got the flood of `command not found` messages. Your install itself succeeded; the paste got dirty afterward. Your log shows Cockpit installed successfully and the socket symlink was created before the garbage commands started. fileciteturn12file0
> 
> Use this rule from now on:
> 
> - paste **only commands**
> - never paste lines that include the shell prompt like `qiadmin@qiserver:~$`
> - never paste command output back into the terminal
> 
> If you still want the prettier Tailscale Serve URL later, the corrected command is:
> 
> ```bash
> sudo tailscale serve --bg --https=9444 https+insecure://127.0.0.1:9090
> ```
> 
> That works because `--bg` and `--https=9444` are flags placed before the target, and the target can be a full URL. citeturn507397search0turn507397search2
> 
> So your immediate next steps are:
> 
> 1. Open `https://100.121.111.106:9090`
> 2. Confirm Cockpit login works
> 3. Then install Portainer for Docker admin
> 4. Then install Paperless for the mail pile
> 
> If Cockpit does not load in the browser, run this one command and paste the output:
> 
> ```bash
> sudo systemctl status cockpit.socket --no-pager
> ```

### Assistant
> Do **not** make the Docker Homepage on your server the **only** door.
> 
> That would be a mistake.
> 
> If your **server** goes down, your door dies.  
> If your **home network** goes down, your door dies.  
> If your **main desktop** goes down, anything hosted there dies.
> 
> So the right answer is:
> 
> ## Final architecture
> 
> ### Canonical one door
> **Cloud-hosted static dashboard**  
> Host this on **Cloudflare Pages**.
> 
> Cloudflare Pages can deploy a plain static HTML site and gives you a hosted site on a `*.pages.dev` subdomain automatically. citeturn498594search1
> 
> ### Secondary internal dashboard
> **Homepage in Docker on the server**  
> Use this only as the **server-aware internal dashboard** when the server is up.
> 
> Homepage is a static, fast dashboard that can be configured with YAML and Docker/service integrations, and its Docker install supports a config mount plus optional read-only Docker socket integration. citeturn498594search2turn498594search6
> 
> ## What this solves
> 
> ### If your main desktop dies
> Your cloud door still works.  
> Your server door still works if the server is up.
> 
> ### If your server dies
> Your cloud door still works for:
> - external web apps
> - docs
> - bookmarks
> - status notes
> - recovery links
> 
> ### If your home Wi-Fi/server internet dies
> Your cloud door still works from phone/mobile data.  
> Your server apps will be unavailable, but the **door** is still there.
> 
> That is the difference between:
> - **door**
> - and **destinations behind the door**
> 
> You were about to tie those together. Don’t.
> 
> ## What to host where
> 
> ### Cloudflare Pages: canonical front door
> Put only:
> - links
> - categories
> - light notes
> - recovery instructions
> - maybe public-safe status text
> - links to external SaaS/web tools
> - links to server services
> - “if this is down, go here” instructions
> 
> Do **not** put:
> - secrets
> - Docker socket anything
> - private API keys
> - deep server widgets that require local access
> 
> ### Server Homepage in Docker: internal ops dashboard
> Put:
> - Open WebUI
> - Cockpit
> - Portainer
> - Paperless
> - Neo4j
> - worker/status widgets
> - Docker-aware service cards
> 
> Because Homepage supports YAML-configured services and widgets, plus Docker integrations if you choose to mount the Docker socket. citeturn498594search0turn498594search6
>

> 
> ### Canonical answer
> **Not on your main PC.**  
> **Not only on your server.**
> 
> Instead:
> 
> - **Primary door:** static dashboard on **Cloudflare Pages**
> - **Optional secondary door:** Homepage Docker container on **your server**
> 
> That gives you one **conceptual** door, but two **delivery layers**:
> - resilient cloud entry
> - rich local/server entry
> 
> That is the only version that actually matches your fear model.
>

>

> Because it stays up independently of your server and desktop, and Cloudflare explicitly supports deploying static HTML sites. citeturn498594search1

### User
> ok, i have cloned the homepage git to to my folder inside qilabs mono repo into _QiAccess_Start, now what.

### Assistant
> Good. Now the blunt part:
> 
> **You cloned the wrong thing for v1.**  
> Do **not** build or run the upstream Homepage source inside your mono repo unless you specifically plan to hack Homepage itself.
> 
> Homepage’s official path is to run the **Docker image** and mount a **config directory** into `/app/config`. It also requires `HOMEPAGE_ALLOWED_HOSTS` for access through anything other than localhost. Homepage is configured with YAML files like `services.yaml` and `widgets.yaml`. citeturn296500search0turn296500search1turn296500search6turn973102search3
> 
> So here’s what to do next.
> 
> ## 1) Fix the repo setup
> 
> If `_QiAccess_Start` is a literal clone of the upstream Homepage repo, don’t keep that nested `.git` inside your mono repo unless you want nested-repo nonsense.
> 
> Do one of these:
> 
> **Best option**
> - delete that clone
> - recreate `_QiAccess_Start` as **your** project folder
> 
> **Acceptable option**
> - keep the folder
> - remove its inner `.git`
> - use it only for your config and deployment files
> 
> From your main machine, inside `_QiAccess_Start`:
> 
> ```bash
> rm -rf .git
> mkdir -p homepage-local/config
> ```
> 
> ## 2) Make `_QiAccess_Start` your config project, not the Homepage source
> 
> Use this structure:
> 
> ```text
> _QiAccess_Start/
>   homepage-local/
>     docker-compose.yml
>     config/
>       services.yaml
>       widgets.yaml
>       bookmarks.yaml
> ```
> 
> That gives you one clean place to version:
> - the door layout
> - the links
> - the widgets
> - the deployment file
> 
> ## 3) Create the local Homepage Docker stack
> 
> Put this in:
> 
> ```text
> _QiAccess_Start/homepage-local/docker-compose.yml
> ```
> 
> ```yaml
> services:
>   homepage:
>     image: ghcr.io/gethomepage/homepage:latest
>     container_name: homepage
>     restart: unless-stopped
>     ports:
>       - "127.0.0.1:3001:3000"
>     volumes:
>       - ./config:/app/config
>       - /var/run/docker.sock:/var/run/docker.sock:ro
>     environment:
>       HOMEPAGE_ALLOWED_HOSTS: localhost:3001,127.0.0.1:3001,100.121.111.106:3001,qiserver-1.cerberus-sirius.ts.net,qiserver-1.cerberus-sirius.ts.net:3001
> ```
> 
> Why this shape:
> - Docker image is the supported install path citeturn296500search0turn296500search1
> - `/app/config` is where Homepage reads config from citeturn296500search0
> - Docker socket is optional, but useful if you want Docker-aware integrations later citeturn296500search0turn296500search4
> - `HOMEPAGE_ALLOWED_HOSTS` is required for non-localhost access citeturn296500search1
> 
> ## 4) Create the first real door config
> 
> Put this in:
> 
> ```text
> _QiAccess_Start/homepage-local/config/services.yaml
> ```
> 
> ```yaml
> - Door:
>     - AI Chat:
>         href: https://qiserver-1.cerberus-sirius.ts.net
>         description: Open WebUI
> 
>     - Host Admin:
>         href: https://100.121.111.106:9090
>         description: Cockpit
> 
>     - Docker Admin:
>         href: https://100.121.111.106:9443
>         description: Portainer
> 
> - Work:
>     - Mail Intake:
>         href: https://100.121.111.106:8000
>         description: Paperless (planned)
> 
>     - Qi Queue:
>         href: https://100.121.111.106:8001
>         description: Local control plane (planned)
> 
> - Memory:
>     - Runbook:
>         href: https://qiserver-1.cerberus-sirius.ts.net
>         description: Start here if lost
> 
>     - Server Status:
>         href: https://100.121.111.106:9090
>         description: Host health and services
> ```
> 
> Put this in:
> 
> ```text
> _QiAccess_Start/homepage-local/config/widgets.yaml
> ```
> 
> ```yaml
> - datetime:
>     text_size: xl
>     format:
>       timeStyle: short
> 
> - resources:
>     cpu: true
>     memory: true
> ```
> 
> Homepage supports YAML config, `services.yaml` groups, and info widgets via `widgets.yaml`. The `datetime` and `resources` widget syntax above is directly aligned with the docs. citeturn296500search6turn973102search4turn973102search2turn973102search3
> 
> Put this in:
> 
> ```text
> _QiAccess_Start/homepage-local/config/bookmarks.yaml
> ```
> 
> ```yaml
> - Quick Links:
>     - GitHub:
>         - href: https://github.com
> 
>     - Cloudflare:
>         - href: https://dash.cloudflare.com
> 
>     - Supabase:
>         - href: https://supabase.com/dashboard
> ```
> 
> ## 5) Do not switch your main door yet
> 
> Right now your root Tailscale door is still Open WebUI.
>

> 
> First:
> - get Homepage running locally on `127.0.0.1:3001`
> - verify it looks right
> - then we switch the root door from Open WebUI to Homepage
> 
> Do **not** change the front door before the new door exists.
> 
> ## 6) Copy this stack to the server and run it there
> 
> On the server, create:
> 
> ```bash
> mkdir -p /srv/qios/stacks/homepage
> ```
> 
> Then copy the contents of `_QiAccess_Start/homepage-local/` into:
> 
> ```text
> /srv/qios/stacks/homepage/
> ```
> 
> Once copied, on the server run:
> 
> ```bash
> cd /srv/qios/stacks/homepage
> docker compose up -d
> docker ps
> ```
> 
> Then test locally on the server:
> 
> ```bash
> curl -I http://127.0.0.1:3001
> ```
> 
> ## 7) After it works, make it the root door
> 
> Right now your Tailscale Serve root points to Open WebUI.
> 
> Once Homepage is working on port `3001`, change the root to Homepage and move Open WebUI to a separate HTTPS port.
> 
> Use this sequence on the server:
> 
> ```bash
> sudo tailscale serve reset
> sudo tailscale serve --bg 3001
> sudo tailscale serve --bg --https=9446 3000
> tailscale serve status
> ```
> 
> The Serve CLI supports:
> - `tailscale serve [flags] <target>`
> - `--https=<port>`
> - port, partial URL, or full URL targets citeturn224472search0turn224472search3
> 
> That gives you:
> 
> - **Root door:** `https://qiserver-1.cerberus-sirius.ts.net` → Homepage
> - **AI chat:** `https://qiserver-1.cerberus-sirius.ts.net:9446` → Open WebUI
> 
> That is the correct split.
> 
> ## 8) The correct mental model
> 
> From now on:
> 
> - `_QiAccess_Start` = your **door config project**
> - `/srv/qios/stacks/homepage` = the **running local door**
> - Homepage = the **launcher/status layer**
> - not the backend
> - not the canonical data source
> - not the pipeline
> 
> ## What to do right now
> 
> In order:
> 
> 1. strip the nested `.git` from `_QiAccess_Start`
> 2. create `homepage-local/docker-compose.yml`
> 3. create the three YAML config files
> 4. copy that folder to `/srv/qios/stacks/homepage`
> 5. run `docker compose up -d`
> 6. verify `http://127.0.0.1:3001`
> 7. then switch Tailscale root from Open WebUI to Homepage
> 
> If you want, next I’ll give you the exact `scp` command to move `_QiAccess_Start/homepage-local` from your main machine to the server, then the exact verify commands after it starts.

> 
> I changed **AI Chat** to use `:9446` instead of root because the plan is to make **Homepage the root door** and move Open WebUI to a side port. That keeps the mental model clean:
> - root = door
> - `:9446` = AI room
> 
> ## What goes to the server
> 
> After you update the repo root files on your PC, copy only these to the server:
> 
> - `docker-compose.yml`
> - `config/`
> 
> To the server path:
>

> /srv/qios/stacks/homepage/
> ```

> 
> ```powershell

> scp -r "C:\QiLabs\_QiAccess_Start\config" qiadmin@100.121.111.106:/srv/qios/stacks/homepage/

> 
> ```bash
> cd /srv/qios/stacks/homepage
> docker compose up -d
> docker ps
> curl -I http://127.0.0.1:3001
> ```
> 
> ## Then switch the root door
> 
> After Homepage is confirmed on port `3001`:
> 
> ```bash
> sudo tailscale serve reset
> sudo tailscale serve --bg 3001
> sudo tailscale serve --bg --https=9446 3000
> tailscale serve status
> ```
> 
> Then you’ll have:
> 
> - `https://qiserver-1.cerberus-sirius.ts.net` → QiAccess door
> - `https://qiserver-1.cerberus-sirius.ts.net:9446` → Open WebUI
> 
> ## Cloudflare piece
> 
> Do **not** try to use this Homepage repo as the Cloudflare Pages site too.
> 
> That is the trap.
> 
> Use:
> - **Homepage repo** = local server door
> - **separate simple static Cloudflare Pages site** = fallback door
> 
> The fallback cloud door should just be a lightweight static launcher with:
> - external web apps
> - status text
> - recovery instructions
> - links to the server apps when available
> 
> That way if the server dies, your door still exists.
> 
> ## So the build order is
> 
> 1. update root repo config files
> 2. copy to server
> 3. run Homepage locally on server
> 4. switch Tailscale root to Homepage
> 5. only then build the separate Cloudflare fallback door
>

### Assistant
> You ran **Windows copy commands inside the Ubuntu shell**. That’s what blew up.
> 
> ## What happened
> 
> You were already logged into the server:
> 
> ```text
> qiadmin@qiserver:~$
> ```
> 
> Then you pasted this:
> 
> ```bash
> scp "C:\QiLabs\_QiAccess_Start\docker-compose.yml" ...
> scp -r "C:\QiLabs\_QiAccess_Start\config" ...
> ```
> 
> On **Linux**, `C:\...` is not a valid local path. The shell treated `C:` like a hostname-ish token, which is why you got:
> 
> ```text
> ssh: Could not resolve hostname c: Temporary failure in name resolution
> ```
> 
> So the failure was:
> 
> - you were on the **server**
> - but trying to copy files from your **Windows PC**
> - using a **Windows path**
> - from the wrong machine
> 
> That cannot work.
> 
> ## The other things you saw are normal
> 
> These are not problems:
> 
> - **Kernel changed** from `6.17.0-20` to `6.17.0-22`  
>   That just means updates were installed and the machine rebooted.
> 
> - **Local LAN IP changed** from `192.168.0.78` to `192.168.0.102`  
>   Normal DHCP behavior.
> 
> - **Web console shows `https://qiserver:9090/ or https://192.168.0.102:9090/`**  
>   That’s just Cockpit advertising local access methods.
> 
> - **Host authenticity / known_hosts warning**  
>   Normal because SSH knows the same box under multiple names/addresses.
> 
> ## What you should do instead
> 
> ### Rule
> - **If the source files are on your Windows PC**, run `scp` from **Windows**
> - **If the source files are already on the server**, use `cp` on **Linux**
> 
> Right now your source files are on **Windows**, so do this from your **main desktop**, not from the server SSH session.
> 
> ## Correct workflow
> 
> ### Step 1 — leave the server session alone
> You can keep it open, but don’t run the Windows `scp` commands there.
> 
> ### Step 2 — open PowerShell or Command Prompt on your main PC
> 
> Run this there:
> 
> ```powershell
> scp "C:\QiLabs\_QiAccess_Start\docker-compose.yml" qiadmin@100.121.111.106:/srv/qios/stacks/homepage/
> scp -r "C:\QiLabs\_QiAccess_Start\config" qiadmin@100.121.111.106:/srv/qios/stacks/homepage/
> ```
> 
> That is the right place for those commands.
> 
> ### Step 3 — back on the server, verify
> 
> ```bash
> ls -R /srv/qios/stacks/homepage
> ```
> 
> Then:
>

> cd /srv/qios/stacks/homepage
> docker compose up -d
> docker ps

> ## If you want to do it from the server anyway

> Two likely things happened, and one of them is almost certainly real:
> 
> ## What happened
> 
> ### 1) You hit Homepage too early
> Your container is:
> 
> ```text
> Up Less than a second (health: starting)
> ```
> 
> So `curl` right then was premature.  
> That exact `Recv failure: Connection reset by peer` is common when the container is still coming up and the app isn’t ready yet.
> 
> ### 2) Your `config/` copy probably did **not** complete
> This is the bigger concern.
> 
> In PowerShell, I only see the first `scp` finishing:
> 
> ```text
> docker-compose.yml 100%
> ```
> 
> Then the second command:
> 
> ```powershell
> scp -r "C:\QiLabs\_QiAccess_Start\config" qiadmin@100.121.111.106:/srv/qios/stacks/homepage/
> ```
> 
> gets immediately followed by your **server shell output**, not an `scp` completion line.
> 
> That usually means:
> - you switched contexts before it finished, or
> - it didn’t actually complete cleanly
> 
> So right now there’s a decent chance the server has:
> - `docker-compose.yml`
> - but **no real config folder**, or only a partial one
> 
> If that happened, Docker still started Homepage with an empty/default-mounted config path, which can absolutely make startup weird.
> 
> ## Do this now on the server

>

> Here’s what happened:
> 
> **Homepage itself is fine. Your config copy is not.**
> 
> The container is healthy and serving, but the server only has a **partial config** right now: just `settings.yaml`, `kubernetes.yaml`, and a `logs/` folder. Your `services.yaml`, `bookmarks.yaml`, `widgets.yaml`, `docker.yaml`, and `proxmox.yaml` never made it over. The Homepage logs also show it auto-copied `settings.yaml` and `kubernetes.yaml` into the mounted config folder on startup. fileciteturn23file0
> 
> And your Windows `scp` output confirms the real failure: multiple files hit **`Permission denied`** when trying to write into `/srv/qios/stacks/homepage/config/`, including `bookmarks.yaml`, `docker.yaml`, `services.yaml`, and `widgets.yaml`. fileciteturn23file1 fileciteturn23file5
> 
> So the sequence was:
> 
> 1. `docker-compose.yml` copied successfully. fileciteturn23file1  
> 2. Homepage started.  
> 3. Homepage created some default config files in the mounted config directory. fileciteturn23file0  
> 4. Your later `scp -r config` hit permission errors and only got a couple files through. fileciteturn23file1  
> 5. Result: Homepage runs, but it’s basically running with a crippled/default config.
>

> 
> Do this on the **server** first:
>

> cd /srv/qios/stacks/homepage
> docker compose down
>

> mkdir -p /srv/qios/stacks/homepage/config
> sudo chown -R qiadmin:qiadmin /srv/qios/stacks/homepage
> chmod -R u+rwX /srv/qios/stacks/homepage
> ```
> 
> That wipes the bad partial config and gives `qiadmin` clean ownership.
> 
> Then from **Windows PowerShell** rerun the copy:
> 
> ```powershell
> scp -r "C:\QiLabs\_QiAccess_Start\config" qiadmin@100.121.111.106:/srv/qios/stacks/homepage/

> ## What actually happened
> 
> ### 1) Homepage works

>

> ```
>

> 
> ### 2) Your main machine got “refused connection” because you bound Homepage to localhost only
> Your compose maps:
> 
> ```text
> 127.0.0.1:3001->3000
> ```
> 
> That means **only the server itself** can reach port `3001` directly.
> 
> So from your main PC:
> - `http://100.121.111.106:3001` will fail
> - because the service is **not listening on the server’s network interface**
> - it is only listening on `127.0.0.1` inside that machine
> 
> That part is intentional and safer.
> 
> ### 3) You pasted output back into bash again
> That’s why you got garbage like:
> 
> - `[+]: command not found`
> - `✔: command not found`
> - `Skipping: command not found`
> 
> You pasted terminal output into the shell, and bash tried to execute it.
> 
> Also:
> 
> ```bash
> http://127.0.0.1:3001
> ```
> 
> is **not a command**. That’s a URL.  
> Use it in a browser, or use `curl`.
>

>

> 2. **Homepage does not like the proxied Host header**
> 3. **Your config is valid enough to boot, but something in the rendered request path is erroring under the external hostname**
>

>

> - Homepage breaks only when the Tailscale hostname is used
> - or Tailscale Serve itself is pointing somewhere stupid

> ## One correction to the mental model
> 
> You do **not** need a desktop app here.  
> For this layer, you want:
> 
> - **local web door** on the server = Homepage
> - **AI room** = Open WebUI on `:9446`
> - **host admin** = Cockpit
> - **Docker admin** = Portainer
> - later **cloud fallback door** as a separate static site
> 
> That keeps the “one door” concept while not tying your only door to one fragile runtime.

### User
> ## AI

> 

> ### Analyzing network data

> 

> **Request:**

> ````

> Request URL: https://qiserver-1.cerberus-sirius.ts.net/

> 

> Request headers:

> DNT: 1

> Upgrade-Insecure-Requests: 1

> User-Agent: Mozilla/5.0 (Windows NT 10.0; Win64; x64) AppleWebKit/537.36 (KHTML, like Gecko) Chrome/147.0.0.0 Safari/537.36

> sec-ch-ua: <redacted>

> sec-ch-ua-mobile: <redacted>

> sec-ch-ua-platform: <redacted>

> ````

> 

> **Response:**

> ````

> Response headers:

> accept-ranges: bytes

> content-encoding: zstd

> content-length: <redacted>

> content-type: text/html; charset=utf-8

> date: Mon, 20 Apr 2026 18:30:15 GMT

> etag: <redacted>

> last-modified: Fri, 27 Mar 2026 00:30:19 GMT

> server: uvicorn

> vary: Accept-Encoding

> x-process-time: <redacted>

> 

> Response status: 200 

> Network request status: finished, preserved

> ````

> 

> **Timing:**

> ````

> Queued at (timestamp): 13.4 s

> Started at (timestamp): 13.4 s

> Queueing (duration): 9 ms

> Connection start (stalled) (duration): 11 ms

> Request sent (duration): 0 s

> Waiting for server response (duration): 19 ms

> Content download (duration): 2 ms

> Duration (duration): 42 ms

> ````

> 

> **Request initiator chain:**

> ````

> - URL: https://qiserver-1.cerberus-sirius.ts.net/

> ````

> 

> ### Answer

> 

> Based on the data provided, the request is **not actually failing**. 

> 

> The **Response status: 200** and **Network request status: finished** indicate that the HTTP transaction was successful. The server received the request and successfully returned a document (likely an HTML landing page).

> 

> However, if you are experiencing an issue where the page isn't loading as expected in the browser, here are the technical factors to consider:

> 

> ### 1. Tailscale/VPN Connectivity

> The domain `ts.net` indicates this is a **Tailscale MagicDNS** address. 

> * **Potential Issue:** If your Tailscale client is disconnected or you lose access to the "cerberus-sirius" tailnet, the browser will fail to resolve this DNS name or establish a connection, even if previous requests were successful.

> 

> ### 2. Analysis of Performance & Timing

> The timing data shows a very healthy request-response cycle:

> * **Waiting (TTFB):** 19ms. This is excellent, suggesting the `uvicorn` server processed the logic quickly (confirmed by the presence of the `x-process-time` header).

> * **Content Download:** 2ms. The payload is small and transmitted rapidly.

> * **Total Duration:** 42ms. There are no network bottlenecks here.

> 

> ### 3. Possible "Soft" Failures

> If the page appears blank or broken despite the 200 OK status, check the following:

> * **Empty Response:** The `content-length` is redacted here, but if the actual body is 0 bytes, the server may be misconfigured.

> * **Sub-resource Failures:** The initial document (`/`) loaded fine, but check the **Console** or other rows in the **Network tab** for failed CSS, JavaScript, or API calls that the HTML might be trying to load.

> * **Uvicorn/FastAPI Errors:** Since the server header is `uvicorn`, if this is a development environment, ensure the application hasn't entered an error state that returns an empty success page.

> 

> **Summary:** From a network protocol perspective, this specific request succeeded. If you are seeing a "failure," it is likely happening in the **rendering** of the page or in **subsequent requests** triggered by this HTML.

### Assistant
> That AI answer is mostly right, but it missed the **important clue**.
> 
> ## What this actually shows
> 
> That request did **not** fail.
> 
> It returned:
> 
> - **200 OK**
> - `server: uvicorn`
> - `x-process-time`
> - `last-modified: Fri, 27 Mar 2026 00:30:19 GMT`
> 
> Those headers are the fingerprint of **Open WebUI**, not Homepage.
> 
> ### Why that matters
> When Homepage is serving, you were seeing headers like:
> 
> - `X-Powered-By: Next.js`
> - `x-nextjs-cache`
> 
> When Open WebUI is serving, you see:
> 
> - `server: uvicorn`
> - `x-process-time`
> 
> So this network capture proves:
> 
> **At the moment you captured it, the root URL was serving Open WebUI, not Homepage.**
> 
> ## So what happened?
> 
> One of these is true:
> 
> 1. you captured that request **before** the Tailscale Serve root switch fully took effect, or
> 2. the browser was still looking at an older page/session, or
> 3. you were testing at a time when root still pointed to Open WebUI
> 
> But the key point is:
> 
> **This request is not evidence of Homepage failing.**
> It is evidence that the browser got a valid HTML response from the **wrong app** for the root door.
> 
> ## The clean interpretation
> 
> - `https://qiserver-1.cerberus-sirius.ts.net/`
>   returned **Open WebUI HTML**
> - not Homepage HTML
> - and it returned it successfully
> 
> So the earlier browser weirdness was **not a network outage**
> and **not a Tailscale connectivity problem**
> 
> It was a **routing / stale-client-state / wrong-surface** issue.
> 
> ## How to verify the difference fast
> 
> From your main machine:
> 
> ### Root should be Homepage
> If root is Homepage, headers should look like:
> - `X-Powered-By: Next.js`
> - `x-nextjs-cache`
> 
> ### AI should be Open WebUI
> If `:9446` is Open WebUI, headers should look like:
> - `server: uvicorn`
> - `x-process-time`
> 
> That’s the easiest fingerprint check.
> 
> ## What to do now
> 
> ### 1. Hard refresh or use incognito
> Your browser is probably holding onto stale state.
> 
> ### 2. Re-test both URLs
> - `https://qiserver-1.cerberus-sirius.ts.net`
> - `https://qiserver-1.cerberus-sirius.ts.net:9446`
> 
> ### 3. Compare headers again
> If root still shows `server: uvicorn`, then root is still effectively landing on Open WebUI from the client’s perspective.
> 
> ### 4. On the server, confirm current routing
> Run:
> 
> ```bash
> tailscale serve status
> ```
> 
> You want it to show:
> - root → `http://127.0.0.1:3001`
> - `:9446` → `http://127.0.0.1:3000`
> 
> ## The blunt answer
> 
> That network capture means:
> 
> **the browser successfully got Open WebUI at the root URL.**
> Not a failure.
> Just the wrong destination for the “one door.”
> 
> If you want, the next move is I help you do one final clean verification pass:
> - curl root
> - curl `:9446`
> - browser incognito check
> - confirm which app is actually at each endpoint.

> ### `custom.js`
> 
> ```javascript
> document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", () => {
>   // Title + meta branding
>   document.title = "QiAccess";
> 
>   let metaDesc = document.querySelector('meta[name="description"]');
>   if (!metaDesc) {
>     metaDesc = document.createElement("meta");
>     metaDesc.setAttribute("name", "description");
>     document.head.appendChild(metaDesc);
>   }
>   metaDesc.setAttribute("content", "QiAccess — one door to Cody's stack.");
> 
>   // Optional: soften stock branding if it appears anywhere obvious
>   const footerVersionLinks = document.querySelectorAll('#version a');
>   footerVersionLinks.forEach((el) => {
>     el.setAttribute("title", "Homepage engine");
>   });
> 
>   // If the search widget label leaks visually, force-hide the SR-only text
>   document.querySelectorAll(".sr-only").forEach((el) => {
>     el.setAttribute("aria-hidden", "true");
>   });
> });
> ```
> 
> ### `custom.css`
> 
> ```css
> /* QiAccess polish */
> :root {
>   --qi-radius: 14px;
> }
> 
> /* Make the top area feel more like a dashboard header */
> #information-widgets {
>   backdrop-filter: blur(8px);
> }
> 
> /* Slightly cleaner card feel */
> .service-card,
> .bookmark a,
> .bookmark a:visited {
>   border-radius: var(--qi-radius);
> }
> 
> /* Tighten headings a bit */
> .service-group-name,
> .bookmark-group-name {
>   letter-spacing: 0.01em;
> }
> 
> /* Hide any screen-reader-only label that somehow leaks visibly */
> .sr-only {
>   position: absolute !important;
>   width: 1px !important;
>   height: 1px !important;
>   padding: 0 !important;
>   margin: -1px !important;
>   overflow: hidden !important;
>   clip: rect(0, 0, 0, 0) !important;
>   white-space: nowrap !important;
>   border: 0 !important;
> }
> 
> /* Specifically nuke the stray visible search label case */
> .information-widget-search .sr-only {
>   display: none !important;
> }
> 
> /* Optional: de-emphasize engine/version footer */
> #version {
>   opacity: 0.65;
> }
> 
> /* Make service descriptions a little clearer */
> .service-description,
> .bookmark-description {
>   opacity: 0.9;
> }
> ```

> 
> ## Final answer
> 
> ### Rename the server stack folder to:
> ```text
> /srv/qios/stacks/_qiaccess_start/
> ```
> 
> That is better than:
> 
> ```text
> /srv/qios/stacks/homepage/
> ```
> 
> because `homepage` is the engine, but `_qiaccess_start` is **your app/deployment identity**.
> 
> ## But no, do not copy the entire source repo to the server
> 
> That part is the important correction.
> 
> Since you are running the **Homepage Docker image**, the app code already lives **inside the container image**.  
> So you do **not** need to copy:
> 
> - `src/`
> - `pages/`
> - `components/`
> - docs
> - package files
> - the whole repo
> 
> That would just create confusion.
> 
> ## What you *should* do
> 
> Use a **runtime mirror**, not a full source mirror.
> 
> ### Canonical dev/source on your PC
> ```text
> C:\QiLabs\_QiAccess_Start\
> ```
> 
> ### Canonical runtime on the server
> ```text
> /srv/qios/stacks/_qiaccess_start/
> ```
> 
> And inside the server runtime folder, keep only what the container actually needs at runtime.
> 
> ## Best structure
> 
> ```text
> /srv/qios/stacks/_qiaccess_start/
> ├── docker-compose.yml
> └── config/
>     ├── settings.yaml
>     ├── services.yaml
>     ├── widgets.yaml
>     ├── bookmarks.yaml
>     ├── docker.yaml
>     ├── proxmox.yaml
>     ├── kubernetes.yaml
>     ├── custom.css
>     ├── custom.js
>     ├── logs/
>     └── images/
>         ├── banner_dark@2x.png
>         ├── banner_dark@2x.webp
>         ├── banner_light@2x.png
>         ├── banner_light@2x.webp
>         ├── blossom_valley.jpg
>         ├── blossom_valley_blur.jpg
>         ├── favicon.ico
>         ├── light_squircle@2x.png
>         ├── light_squircle@2x.webp
>         ├── sections.png
>         ├── sections.webp
>         └── widget_stocks_demo.png
> ```
> 
> ## Why this is the right move
> 
> Because right now your Docker compose mounts only:
> 
> ```yaml
> ./config:/app/config
> ```
> 
> So anything **outside** `config/` is **not visible inside the container**.
> 
> That means if your custom CSS/JS or Homepage config references images, and those images are sitting somewhere else in the repo, the container will not see them unless you:
> 
> - move them under `config/`, or
> - add another volume mount
> 
> For ADHD consistency and lower friction, the cleanest move is:
> 
> ## Put all runtime assets under `config/`
> 
> That way:
> - one mount
> - one deploy bundle
> - one predictable mental model
> 
> ## So yes, your instinct is correct
> 
> Not “copy the whole repo.”
> 
> But:
> 
> - **rename the server folder to `_qiaccess_start`**
> - **copy the full runtime bundle**
> - **include the image assets in the runtime bundle**
> - **keep runtime assets under `config/` so the container can actually use them**
> 
> ## What I recommend doing now
> 
> ### 1. Rename server folder
> On the server:
> 
> ```bash
> mv /srv/qios/stacks/homepage /srv/qios/stacks/_qiaccess_start
> ```
>

> From now on, deploy to:
> 
> ```text
> /srv/qios/stacks/_qiaccess_start/
> ```
> 
> ### 3. Move or copy your image assets into:
> ```text
> C:\QiLabs\_QiAccess_Start\config\images\
> ```
> 
> ### 4. Copy that same structure to the server
> So the runtime side matches.
>

> ### Source project
> `_QiAccess_Start` = your editable source/deploy config project

> `/srv/qios/stacks/_qiaccess_start` = deployed runtime bundle

>

### User
> here is the url to the cloudflare deployment, https://qiaccess-start.pages.dev/, also i added custom domain access.qially.com how do i add the zero trust.

### Assistant
> You’ve got two valid choices.
> 
> **Best architecture:** leave `access.qially.com` public as the real front door, and put Zero Trust only on the private/ops destinations behind it.  
> **Locked-down architecture:** put Cloudflare Access directly in front of `access.qially.com`.
> 
> Why I’m saying that: Cloudflare Access is an authentication layer for a public hostname, added as an **Access application**. For a public site, you add a **Self-hosted** app, then add a **public hostname** for the domain you want to protect. Cloudflare checks each HTTP request for a valid application token and sends unauthenticated users to the Access login flow. citeturn996343search0turn996343search3turn996343search2
> 
> Since you already attached `access.qially.com` to your Pages project, that hostname is the thing you’d secure. Pages custom domains are configured under **Workers & Pages → your project → Custom domains**. citeturn803580search0
> 
> ## My recommendation
> 
> Make `access.qially.com` **public** and use it as orientation/recovery.  
> Then protect things like:
> 
> - `local.access.qially.com`
> - `ops.access.qially.com`
> - `ai.access.qially.com`
> 
> with Access.
> 
> That keeps your front door always reachable, while the sensitive rooms stay behind Zero Trust.
> 
> ## If you want Zero Trust on `access.qially.com` itself
> 
> Do this in Cloudflare:
> 
> 1. Go to **Cloudflare One / Zero Trust → Access controls → Applications**.  
> 2. Click **Add an application**.  
> 3. Choose **Self-hosted**.  
> 4. Name it something like `QiAccess Web`.  
> 5. Set a **Session Duration**.  
> 6. Click **Add public hostname**.  
> 7. Choose the domain and set the hostname to `access.qially.com`. Cloudflare’s docs note that public destinations can be specific hostnames and can also include paths or wildcards when needed. citeturn996343search0turn996343search3turn996343search2  
> 8. Create an **Allow** policy. Access policies require at least one **Include** rule, and the usual safe choice is specific emails or an email domain. citeturn407589search0turn407589search1
> 
> A simple safe policy is:
> 
> - **Action:** Allow
> - **Include:** your exact email address, or your email domain
> 
> Do **not** use **Include Everyone**, and do **not** use **Include Login Methods: One-time PIN** by itself, because Cloudflare explicitly calls those common misconfigurations that can make the app reachable to anybody who matches that broad rule. citeturn407589search0turn407589search1
> 
> ## If you want email-code login instead of Google/Okta/etc.
> 
> Cloudflare supports **One-time PIN** as an identity provider. You add it under **Zero Trust → Integrations → Identity providers → Add new identity provider → One-time PIN**, then grant access by putting the approved email addresses in the Access policy. citeturn407589search2turn407589search4
> 
> That’s the fastest setup if this is mostly for you.
> 
> ## The cleaner split for your setup
> 
> I’d do this:
> 
> - `access.qially.com` → public front door, no Access
> - `local.access.qially.com` → protected with Access
> - `ai.access.qially.com` → protected with Access
> - `ops.access.qially.com` → protected with Access
> 
> That gives you:
> - one always-available front door
> - protected operational surfaces
> - less friction from your phone when you just need orientation
> 
> ## One more useful detail
> 
> Cloudflare’s newer Access model supports **public destinations** and says those URIs can include a **domain and path with wildcards**, so later you can protect only part of a site if you want, like `access.qially.com/local/*`, instead of the whole hostname. citeturn803580search8turn996343search2
> 
> ## The short version
> 
> If this domain is your **actual front door**, keep it public.  
> If this domain is meant to be **private**, add an Access self-hosted application for `access.qially.com` and an Allow policy for your email.
> 
> If you want, I’ll map the exact subdomain layout I’d use for `access.qially.com`, `local`, `ai`, and `ops` so you can wire Cloudflare Pages + Zero Trust without painting yourself into a corner.

### User
> but i already made the app and this is trying to get me to create the app again, also if cloudflare zero trust has an app launcher, why dont we set up the app launcher that allows us to add other things there as well or is that a bad idea.

### Assistant
> You do **not** need to create the app again.
> 
> If you already made the Access application, just **edit the existing application** and turn on its App Launcher visibility. In Cloudflare Access, tiles have a **one-to-one relationship with applications**, and for an existing app you go to **Access controls → Applications → select the app → Configure → Experience settings → Show application in App Launcher**. You can also choose which application domain the tile should use and optionally add a custom logo and tags. citeturn996740search0turn137704view2
> 
> So the clean answer is:
> 
> ## What to do with the app you already made
> 
> Use the existing app and adjust it.
> 
> For each app you want in the launcher:
> - open the existing Access app
> - go to **Experience settings**
> - enable **Show application in App Launcher**
> - choose the application domain
> - optionally add a custom logo and tags
> 
> Also, Cloudflare is explicit that this toggle **does not change whether users can reach the app directly**. It only controls whether the app shows as a tile in the launcher. Allowed users can still hit the direct link even if the tile is hidden. citeturn996740search0
> 
> ## Should you use the App Launcher?
> 
> Yes — but as a **private portal**, not as your only “front door.”
> 
> Cloudflare’s App Launcher is a single dashboard at your team domain like `<team>.cloudflareaccess.com`, where users see tiles for applications they are already authorized to use. Access to the launcher itself is controlled by its **own launcher policy**, and those rules are separate from the rules for each protected app. citeturn996740search0
> 
> That makes it useful for:
> - your private ops tools
> - AI/chat
> - server admin
> - internal bookmarks
> - a “members only” access hub
> 
> It is **not** the best replacement for your public-facing QiAccess Web front door, because:
> - it lives on the Cloudflare Access team domain, not your branded public domain by default citeturn996740search0
> - it is authentication-gated by design
> - it is meant to launch protected apps, not act as a general public orientation site
> 
> So the best split is:
> 
> ### Keep this:
> - **QiAccess Web** at `access.qially.com` = public, branded, resilient front door
> 
> ### Add this:
> - **Cloudflare App Launcher** = private internal launcher for protected tools
> 
> That gives you:
> - a public front door
> - a private “house key ring”
> 
> ## Why the App Launcher is still a good idea
> 
> Because once enabled, it becomes a single place where you can add:
> - QiAccess Local
> - AI Chat
> - Cockpit
> - Portainer
> - anything else behind Access
> 
> And users only see tiles for apps they’re actually allowed to use. citeturn996740search0
> 
> That is actually very aligned with your “one place I don’t lose the thread” goal — **for private apps**.
> 
> ## The exact setup I’d recommend
> 
> ### Public
> - `access.qially.com` → your real front door, public site
> 
> ### Private
> - Cloudflare App Launcher → your private launcher for protected apps
> 
> ### Apps to add as tiles
> - QiAccess Local
> - AI Chat
> - Host Admin
> - Docker Admin
> - later Paperless
> - later Queue / Control Plane
> 
> ## How to enable the App Launcher itself
> 
> Cloudflare says the launcher is **disabled by default**. To turn it on, go to **Access controls → Access settings**, open **Manage your App Launcher**, then configure:
> - a **policy** for who can access the launcher
> - the **identity providers** they can use to log in
> 
> Once enabled, the launcher is available at your team domain like `<your-team-name>.cloudflareaccess.com`. Those launcher rules do **not** change permissions for the underlying applications. citeturn996740search0
> 
> ## Important caution
> 
> Do not confuse:
> - **launcher access**
> with
> - **application access**
> 
> They are separate.
> 
> A person can have launcher access but still not see or access an app unless that app’s own Access policy allows them. And turning on “show in launcher” doesn’t make an app more or less reachable directly for someone already allowed. citeturn996740search0
> 
> ## Branding/customization
> 
> Yes, you can customize the App Launcher appearance, but Cloudflare notes that **App Launcher customization is only available on Pay-as-you-go and Enterprise plans**. Customization includes organization name, logo, colors, footer links, and the launcher landing page title/subtitle/image. citeturn996740search1
> 
> So:
> - if you have that plan level, customize it
> - if not, don’t hinge your architecture on heavy launcher branding
> 
> ## Best decision
> 
> Use both:
> 
> - **QiAccess Web** as the real branded front door
> - **Cloudflare App Launcher** as the private authenticated launcher for internal tools
> 
> That is not redundant. It’s clean separation.
> 
> ## What to do next
> 
> 1. Enable the App Launcher if you haven’t already
> 2. Edit your existing Access apps instead of recreating them
> 3. Turn on **Show application in App Launcher** for each one
> 4. Keep `access.qially.com` as your actual public front door
> 
> If you want, I’ll map the exact list of Access apps and subdomains I’d create so the App Launcher and QiAccess Web don’t overlap messily.

### User
> ok so i have access already working with my email where do i update the applaundher to make sure that the app we just created is behind zero access.

### Assistant
> Go to **Cloudflare One → Access controls → Applications**, open the **existing app** you already created, click **Configure**, then use two different places for two different jobs: **Policies** to make sure the app is actually protected by Access, and **Experience settings** to make it show up in the App Launcher. Cloudflare’s docs are explicit that App Launcher tiles have a one-to-one relationship with Access applications, and that the tile is enabled from **Applications → Configure → Experience settings → Show application in App Launcher**. citeturn754545view0
> 
> The important distinction: **App Launcher does not secure the app**. It only controls whether the app appears as a tile. Cloudflare says allowed users can still reach the app directly even if the launcher toggle is off, and blocked users will never have access to the application. So to make sure your app is “behind Zero Trust,” check the app’s **Access policy** on that same application and confirm your email is in an **Allow** policy. Cloudflare also states that all Access policies must contain at least one **Include** rule, which defines the initial pool of eligible users. citeturn754545view0turn754545view2
> 
> So the exact path is:
> 
> 1. **Cloudflare One → Access controls → Applications**  
> 2. Select your existing app → **Configure**  
> 3. In **Policies**, confirm there is an **Allow** policy that includes your email or whatever selector you intended. citeturn754545view2  
> 4. In **Experience settings**, turn on **Show application in App Launcher**. citeturn754545view0  
> 5. In **Application domains**, pick the hostname you want the launcher tile to open. citeturn754545view0
> 
> Separately, if you want the **App Launcher portal itself** available, go to **Access controls → Access settings → Manage your App Launcher** and set a launcher policy plus authentication methods. Cloudflare notes the launcher is disabled by default, has its own policy, and those launcher rules do **not** change permissions for the protected apps themselves. citeturn754545view1
> 
> So the short answer is: **edit the existing app, not a new one**; use **Policies** to secure it, and **Experience settings** to surface it in the launcher.

### User
> i selected bookmarks and the access html site and put it behind my email and tested it in cloudflare all were blocked ubt me, i put it in a private shell and it did not ask me to authenticate. how can i make sure its being blocked for others and is that good.

### Assistant
> What you’re seeing can be **totally normal**.
> 
> If your Access app is set to a **single IdP** with **Instant Auth** enabled, Cloudflare will **skip the Access login page** and redirect straight into the SSO flow instead of prompting on a separate Cloudflare screen. And if **Device authentication identity** is enabled, Cloudflare can also let a user into the app using their **Cloudflare One Client session identity**. So “I was not prompted” does **not** automatically mean the app is public. citeturn243111view1turn253235view0turn253235view2
> 
> Also, your app is still protected if its Access application has an **Allow** policy for only your email, because Access apps are **deny by default** and a user has to match an Allow policy to get in. citeturn243111view1
> 
> Here’s how to verify it properly:
> 
> First, check the app config in Cloudflare:
> - Open the existing Access application.
> - Confirm the **Allow** policy includes only your email or the exact identity selector you want.
> - Check whether **Instant Auth** is on.
> - Check whether **Device authentication identity** is enabled. citeturn243111view1turn253235view0turn253235view2
> 
> Then do a **real unauthenticated test**:
> - Use a browser profile that is **not logged into your IdP**
> - and **not running Cloudflare One/WARP**
> - ideally from a different device or phone on cellular
> - then hit the protected URL
> 
> If the app is protected correctly, Cloudflare should either:
> - send the user into the auth flow, or
> - show the configured block behavior for denied users. Cloudflare lets you choose the denied-user behavior as the default block page, a redirect URL, or a custom block page. citeturn243111view1
> 
> A good quick test is:
> - device A: your normal machine, expected to work
> - device B: phone on mobile data, not signed into your Cloudflare-approved email, expected to get challenged or blocked
> 
> That is a much better test than “private window on my already-trusted machine,” because your machine may still satisfy Access through SSO or device identity. citeturn253235view0turn253235view2
> 
> And yes, this is **good** if your goal is:
> - only you can get in
> - nobody else can
> - you don’t want extra friction every single time
> 
> That’s exactly what Access is for: put Cloudflare authentication between the end user and the origin, and only users who match the Access policy get access. citeturn243111view0turn243111view1
> 
> One important distinction: putting the app in the **App Launcher** does **not** secure it by itself. The launcher only shows tiles for apps the user is already allowed to access; it does not replace the app’s own Access policy. citeturn243111view4turn243111view5
> 
> So the practical answer is:
> 
> - If you want the app to **prompt more visibly**, turn off **Instant Auth**.
> - If you want the app to **stay smooth for you** but still be blocked for others, leave it as-is and test from a device that has neither your SSO session nor your Cloudflare One client identity. citeturn253235view0turn253235view2
> 
> The fastest next check is to open the app from your phone on cellular in a browser where you are **not** logged into the allowed email and confirm you get challenged or blocked.

### User
> so i went to my ipad which is a differnt user and I dont recall ever signign in on it with that email and it still let me in. i enabled the access on the app and its still letting me right in.

### Assistant
> This is the likely problem:
> 
> **You probably protected a Bookmark tile, not the actual website.**
> 
> Cloudflare’s docs are explicit that **Bookmarks are for the App Launcher and do not secure the target application**. They exist so users can see apps in the launcher even when those apps are **not** protected by Access. citeturn962648search4turn962648search0
> 
> So if you chose **Bookmark** for `access.qially.com` or the Pages URL, that would explain exactly what you’re seeing:
> - the tile behavior looks right in Cloudflare
> - policy tests may look right
> - but the actual site still opens directly for anyone
> 
> ## What to check right now
> 
> In **Cloudflare One → Access controls → Applications**, look at the **Type** of the application for your site.
> 
> For the actual website, it needs to be:
> 
> - **Self-hosted**
> 
> Not:
> 
> - **Bookmark**
> 
> Because Bookmark only affects launcher visibility and convenience, not access enforcement. Cloudflare says bookmarks can point to apps that are not secured behind Access. citeturn962648search4turn962648search0
> 
> ## The second likely problem
> 
> Even if you did create a proper **Self-hosted** app for `access.qially.com`, your **Pages production URL** may still be publicly reachable at:
> 
> ```text
> https://qiaccess-start.pages.dev
> ```
> 
> Cloudflare Pages docs say your project remains accessible through the `*.pages.dev` subdomain unless you explicitly deal with it, and they recommend redirecting the `*.pages.dev` URL to your custom domain using **Bulk Redirects**. citeturn962648search1turn962648search2
> 
> So there are two separate surfaces:
> 
> - `access.qially.com`
> - `qiaccess-start.pages.dev`
> 
> If you only protect one, the other can still be the bypass.
> 
> ## What to do
> 
> ### 1. Make sure the website app is a **Self-hosted** Access app
> For the actual site, create or edit the app so the protected domain is:
> 
> ```text
> access.qially.com
> ```
> 
> and the app type is **Self-hosted**.  
> Do **not** rely on a Bookmark app to protect the site. Bookmarks are launcher-only. citeturn962648search4turn962648search0
> 
> ### 2. Keep the Bookmark only if you want a launcher tile
> That’s fine, but think of it as:
> - Bookmark = shortcut
> - Self-hosted app = actual lock on the door
> 
> ### 3. Kill the Pages bypass
> Redirect:
> 
> ```text
> qiaccess-start.pages.dev
> ```
> 
> to:
> 
> ```text
> https://access.qially.com
> ```
> 
> Cloudflare Pages docs explicitly describe using **Bulk Redirects** to redirect the `*.pages.dev` subdomain to your custom domain. citeturn962648search2turn962648search1
> 
> ### 4. Test the right URL
> After that, test **both**:
> - `https://access.qially.com`
> - `https://qiaccess-start.pages.dev`
> 
> The second one should redirect to the first, not load separately.
> 
> ## Important clarification
> 
> Cloudflare’s **preview deployment access policy** only protects preview URLs like random branch hashes. It does **not** protect your main `*.pages.dev` production domain or your custom domain. So if you enabled Pages preview access somewhere, that is not enough for the real site. citeturn962648search5
> 
> ## The clean architecture
> 
> You want:
> 
> - **Self-hosted Access app** on `access.qially.com`
> - optional **Bookmark** tile in App Launcher
> - **Bulk Redirect** from `qiaccess-start.pages.dev` → `https://access.qially.com`
> 
> That closes the obvious bypass.
>

> ### 1. "Cloud Intake" Drop-Zone (High Value)

> *   **The Idea**: When you're away from home and get a document (e.g., an email PDF), you drop it here. 

> *   **How it works**: The web portal uploads it to a **Supabase Bucket** (which is always available). Your local **Qi Queue** worker then monitors that bucket and automatically pulls the file into your local Paperless inbox once your server is online. 

> *   **Benefit**: You can "send files to your house" even if your local server is currently offline or your VPN is acting up.

### User
> ok so what all do we want on this site and one questions this might mean we do need to add one detailed feature but how do i add bookmarks to sites from the browser, would that be one very lite worker with cloudflare db.

### Assistant
> Yes — **one very light Worker + D1** is the right shape **if** you want “add bookmark from browser” without turning the whole front door into an app.
> 
> Cloudflare D1 is a managed serverless SQL database that is meant to be used from Workers and Pages, and Pages/Workers can bind directly to a D1 database. citeturn994505search0turn994505search5
> 
> ## What belongs on the site
> 
> Keep **QiAccess Web** mostly static. It should have only what helps you re-enter fast:
> 
> - **Enter the House**: QiAccess Local, AI Chat, Host Admin, Docker Admin
> - **Cloud Tools**: GitHub, Cloudflare, Supabase, Drive, Tailscale Admin
> - **Local Tools**: clearly marked as server/Tailscale dependent
> - **Current Focus / Resume**: what you’re doing now, next step
> - **Recovery**: what to do if the server or Tailscale is down
> - **Status chips**: local reachable / requires Tailscale / server unavailable
> - **Bookmarks**: either a visible bento section or a link to a bookmark library page
> 
> That keeps the page a **front door**, not a whole apartment complex.
> 
> ## The one dynamic feature that makes sense
> 
> This one:
> 
> **“Add Bookmark” / “Send to House”**
> 
> Not a full bookmark manager on the homepage.  
> Just a lightweight write path.
> 
> ### Best architecture
> - **Static front door** stays static
> - One **tiny Worker endpoint** handles bookmark saves
> - **D1** stores bookmarks
> - A separate page like `/bookmarks` or `/add` can read/write them
> - The write path is protected by **Cloudflare Access**
> 
> Cloudflare Access lets you protect specific paths and more specific paths take precedence over broader ones, so you can keep the main site public while protecting only something like `/add` or `/admin/bookmarks`. citeturn994505search11turn994505search7
> 
> ## My recommendation
> 
> Do it like this:
> 
> ### Public
> - `access.qially.com/` → static front door
> 
> ### Protected
> - `access.qially.com/add` → bookmark capture form
> - `access.qially.com/bookmarks` → bookmark manager/library
> 
> or even cleaner:
> 
> - `save.access.qially.com` → protected add/save UI
> - `access.qially.com` → public front door
> 
> That way:
> - the front door stays lightweight
> - the save flow is private
> - you don’t turn the landing page into a backend-dependent mess
> 
> ## Why D1 is the right DB here
> 
> Because bookmarks are structured data, not blobs:
> - title
> - url
> - tags
> - category
> - notes
> - created_at
> - source
> - pinned/favorite
> - maybe local/cloud flag
> 
> That fits SQL better than trying to fake it with static JSON. Cloudflare’s own docs position D1 as a serverless SQL database for Workers/Pages. citeturn994505search0turn994505search1
> 
> ## How you’d add bookmarks from the browser
> 
> Best options:
> 
> ### 1. Bookmarklet or share-target style flow
> Click a browser bookmarklet that opens:
> 
> ```text
> https://access.qially.com/add?url=...&title=...
> ```
> 
> Then your protected save page pre-fills the form.
> 
> ### 2. Tiny add form
> A simple form with:
> - URL
> - Title
> - Tags
> - Notes
> - Type: cloud / local / reference / recovery
> 
> ### 3. Optional later
> Browser extension.  
> Not needed now.
> 
> ## The cleanest v1
> 
> I would build:
> 
> - **front door** stays static
> - **one protected add page**
> - **one protected bookmarks page**
> - **one Worker API**
> - **one D1 table**
> 
> That’s it.
> 
> ## Minimal schema
> 
> Something like:
> 
> - `id`
> - `title`
> - `url`
> - `description`
> - `category`
> - `tags`
> - `scope` (`cloud`, `local`, `reference`, `recovery`)
> - `created_at`
> - `updated_at`
> - `pinned`
> 
> ## Final answer
> 
> So yes:
> 
> **If you want browser-added bookmarks, use one lightweight Worker + D1.**
> 
> But keep the front door itself mostly static, and treat bookmark saving as a **separate protected utility**, not part of the homepage runtime. That keeps your architecture clean and your ADHD friction low.
> 
> The order I’d use is:
> 
> 1. finish PWA + VPN/local status on the static front door
> 2. add protected **/add** bookmark flow
> 3. add protected **/bookmarks** library
> 4. only later decide whether bookmarks surface on the homepage itself
> 
> If you want, I’ll sketch the exact v1 bookmark architecture and folder layout next.

### User
> ok while the ide works on that why is the server site page still giving a 500 error on the broswer how do we ensure this does not happen.

### Assistant
> Because it’s almost certainly **not the server anymore**.
> 
> You already proved that:
> 
> - `curl.exe` to the server URL returned **200 OK**
> - private/incognito loaded the page
> - the rendered HTML showed the correct Homepage content
> 
> That means the “500 in the browser” is most likely **stale browser state on that origin**, not a live backend failure.
> 
> ## Why this happened
> 
> Because you reused the **same origin** while changing what lived there.
> 
> That root URL used to point at **Open WebUI**, then later got switched to **Homepage**. Browsers can keep weird baggage for the same origin:
> 
> - cached HTML/assets
> - old cookies
> - service worker state
> - stale app shell
> - cached error responses
> - old auth/session assumptions
> 
> So the problem is not “server unstable.”  
> The problem is **same hostname, different app, browser got confused**.
> 
> ## How we make sure this does not happen again
> 
> ### 1. Stop swapping apps on the same origin
> This is the big one.
> 
> Keep each app on a **stable dedicated address** and do not repoint it later.
> 
> For example:
> 
> - **QiAccess Web** → `access.qially.com`
> - **QiAccess Local** → one stable local URL
> - **AI Chat** → one stable local URL
> - **ops/admin** → one stable local URL
> 
> The mistake was letting one hostname play musical chairs.
> 
> ### 2. Clean the broken browser profile once
> On the browser/profile that still shows 500:
> 
> - clear site data for the exact hostname
> - hard refresh
> - if there is a service worker, unregister it
> - test again
> 
> In Chrome/Edge:
> - open DevTools on the broken page
> - go to **Application**
> - use **Clear storage**
> - clear site data
> - if **Service Workers** exists, unregister any for that origin
> - then reload
> 
> If you want the nuclear option, delete cookies/site data for:
> - `qiserver-1.cerberus-sirius.ts.net`
> - any related `ts.net` entry for that exact site
> 
> ### 3. Keep the server-side routes fixed from now on
> Do not keep experimenting by moving root back and forth.
> 
> Your stable mapping should now stay:
> 
> - root local homepage = Homepage
> - `:9446` = Open WebUI
> 
> No more flipping.
> 
> ### 4. Use the web front door as the real front door
> This also helps.
> 
> Now that `access.qially.com` is your actual front door, you do not need to keep living on the raw server URL in normal use.
> 
> That means:
> - less direct interaction with the volatile local hostname
> - less chance of browser state confusion
> - better separation of public door vs local work door
> 
> ## What to do right now
> 
> ### On the broken browser/profile
> Do this once:
> 1. open the bad server page
> 2. open DevTools
> 3. Application → Clear Storage → clear everything for that site
> 4. if service worker exists, unregister it
> 5. close tab
> 6. reopen the URL
> 
> ### Then use the stable URLs only
> Don’t keep using old test URLs and old tabs.
> 
> ## Best long-term fix
> 
> You want **one app per stable address**.
> 
> Bluntly:
> **never reuse the same hostname for a different app again** unless you’re prepared to clear browser state.
> 
> That is how you prevent this class of issue.
> 
> ## My recommendation
> 
> Use this mental model:
> 
> - **Front door:** `access.qially.com`
> - **Kitchen/work door:** stable local Homepage URL
> - **AI room:** stable Open WebUI URL
> - **Ops room:** stable admin URL
> 
> And leave them there.
> 
> If you want, I can help you define the final stable hostname map now so you stop testing off temporary URLs.
